Title:
Where do the UBC go?
Waste management of Used Beverage Cartons in Denmark
Danish Title:
Hvordan skal vi komme af med MDK-affald?
En vurdering af den Danske affaldshåndtering af mad- og drikkekartoner

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Food and beverage cartons (BC) are multilayered packaging materials used to deliver beverages and
liquid foods sustainably and cost-effectively (Schmidt et al., 2022). BCs are primarily made of cardboard,
polyethylene, and, in some cases, aluminum.
Used beverage cartons (UBCs) account for approximately 4% of Danish household waste. They are
considered a low-value product with a short life span that will never be circular, but since 2021, UBCs
have been considered a separate waste fraction for recycling in Denmark.
This study analyzes the waste management of UBCs in Denmark since the introduction of the Danish
government’s new waste management plan in 2020.
The analysis is conducted using observations,
interviews, data collection, a literature review, material flow analysis (MFA), waste sampling, and life
cycle assessment (LCA). The study aims to determine which waste treatment method is the most
environmentally sustainable for the UBCs collected in Denmark.
Most Danish municipalities, waste companies, and other involved stakeholders were contacted to collect
data for the study.
Contact was made with 66 municipalities, nine waste companies, two sorting
plants, and three middlemen in the field of recyclable waste fractions, covering 96 out of the 98 Danish
municipalities.
The literature review served as the basis for the entire study. While most studies agree that BC is more
sustainable than other beverage packaging materials, the environmental impact of UBC waste man-
agement varies significantly among the systems and countries evaluated. Food residues are considered
a problem for waste management in several studies. Consumer behavior is also a key factor in UBC
waste management, as it is typically up to consumers to empty cartons suﬀiciently and sort UBC waste
properly.
As of March 2023, 69 Danish municipalities were collecting UBC from households using four different
collection schemes. Thirteen other municipalities have begun collecting UBC, but only at waste stations,
which is not ideal for this type of waste. A data analysis conducted prior to this study estimated that
the UBC waste generated in Denmark amounts to approximately 32,393 tons, which includes about
20% residues. The main part of UBC was still incinerated in 2022. About 1,648 tons of UBC waste
were collected in 2022, corresponding to a national collection rate of 5% of the UBC waste generated.
The overall recycling rate was estimated at 2.5% of the waste generated, but the recycling rate is highly
uncertain. Based on the average municipal collection rate in 2022, the potential national UBC collection
is 2,615 tons annually but could range between 371 and 17,018 tons.
The distribution of BC types in the waste and the corresponding level of residues in each type were
determined using 5,853 UBCs from RHW and SS waste streams. The following categories were used:
milk, yogurt, cream, plant-based milk cocoa and water, juice and wine.
There was a substantial
difference between the weight and quantity of found milk and yogurt BCs. The differences were more
pronounced in the waste batches from RHW than in SS waste. Milk cartons had the highest occurrence
in terms of weight and quantity. Food cartons were not examined separately due to large differences in
size, volume, and use.
The UBCs from RHW contained more residues than their source-segregated (SS) waste equivalents. Of
the 35 types compared, eight types from SS waste had an average weight that was more than 50% lower
than the equivalent type from RHW.
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
ii
The clean weight of BCs was used to establish a level of residues for each BC type. The clean weight
of the BCs was determined by weighting 70 additional UBCs specifically selected to represent the BC
types with the highest occurrence in the waste sampling. A range of residue levels was determined for
the BC types. A weighted average, based on occurrence in the sampling, was used to estimate residue
levels for each BC use category and an overall residue level of 16–21%.
A comparative LCA was conducted to compare different available waste treatment methods for UBC
collected in Denmark. The LCA compared incineration, fiber recovery in Sweden and Germany (the
Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios, respectively), and recycling of UBC into construction boards in Sweden
(the Recoma scenario). The inventory used is partly based on the waste sampling and MFA results.
The impact assessment showed that each scenario outperformed in several impact categories, thus
making it challenging to evaluate the most environmentally sustainable scenario without weighing the
results. Concerning climate change, the recycling scenarios produced environmental savings compared
to incineration, which resulted in an environmental burden
In general, the different scenarios produce environmental savings in different impact categories. The
Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios account for most of the burdens arising from UBC waste treatment, but
also for significant savings in freshwater eutrophication. Except for the freshwater eutrophication and
freshwater ecotoxicity impact categories, most normalized scenario results are on a scale of -0.25 to 0.05
PE. The substitution of energy and materials in the scenarios significantly impacted the overall results,
and the scenario analysis showed that the modelled energy system greatly influenced the results.
From the reported results and within the range of uncertainties presented, the Recoma scenario appears
to be the most environmentally sustainable option of the methods evaluated.
==PAGE:5==
Resume
Mad- og drikkekartoner (MDK) er kompositmateriale brugt til at opbevare drikkevare og flydende
madvarer på en bæredygtig og omkostningseffektivt måde (Schmidt et al., 2022). MDK er lavet primært
af papfibre, polyetylen, og nogle tilfælde, alufolie.
MDK står for ca. 4 % af det danske husholdningsaffald. MDK er betragtet som et produkt med lav
værdi og kort levetid som vil aldrig være fuldstændig cirkulær, men siden 2021 er MDK blevet betragtet
som en særskilt affaldsfraktion til genanvendelse i Danmark.
Denne undersøgelse analyserer affaldshåndteringen af MDK i Danmark siden regeringen introducerede
”klimaplan for en grøn affaldssektor” i 2020.
Analysen er blevet gjort ved hjælp af observationer,
interviews, litteraturgennemgang, materialestrøms analyse (MFA), affaldsprøveudtagning og livscyk-
lusvurdering (LCA). Undersøgelsen har til formål at afgøre, hvilken affaldsbehandlingsmetode, er den
mest miljømæssigt bæredygtige for MDK indsamlet i Danmark.
De fleste danske kommuner, affaldsselskaber og andre interessenter blev kontaktet for at indhente data
til undersøgelsen. Data er blevet indsamlet fra 66 kommuner, ni affaldsselskaber, to sorteringsanlæg og
tre aftagere af genanvendelige affaldsfraktioner, og hermed dækkes 96 ud af de 98 danske kommuner.
Litteraturgennemgangen blev brugt som grundlag for hele undersøgelsen. Mens de fleste undersøgelser
er enige i at MDK er mere bæredygtigt end andre drikkevareemballager, er der store variationer i
vurdering af miljøpåvirkningen af affaldshåndtering af MDK på tværs af de vurderede systemer og
lande. Madrester anses i flere undersøgelser for at være et problem for affaldshåndtering. Forbrugernes
adfærd er også en vigtig faktor i MDK affaldshåndtering, da det normalt er op til forbrugerne at tømme
kartonerne tilstrækkeligt og at sortere MDK korrekt.
I marts 2023 havde 69 danske kommuner implementeret husstandsindsamling af UBC ved hjælp af
fire forskellige indsamlingsordninger. Yderligere 13 kommuner er begyndt at indsamle MDK, men kun
på genbrugsstationer, hvilket ikke er ideelt for denne type affald. Et forstudie til denne undersøgelse
vurderede at Der blev genererede ca. 32.393 tons MDK-affald i Danmark i 2022, og ude fra indsamlet
data antages 20 % at være madrester. MFA viser at hovedparten af fraktionen blev forbrændt i 2022.
Der blev indsamlet ca. 1.648 tons MDK-affald i 2022, hvilket giver en national indsamlingsprocent på 5
% af MDK-affald. Den reelle genanvendelsesprocent blev anslået i denne undersøgelse til at være 2,5 %
af det genererede affald, men der er høj usikkerhed forbundet med denne procent. På grundlag af den
gennemsnitlige kommunale indsamlingsprocent i 2022 er den potentielle nationale indsamling af MDK
beregnet til 2.615 tons årligt, men den kan være på mellem 371 og 17.018 tons.
Typefordeling af MDK-affaldet og det tilsvarende niveau af madrester i hver type MDK blev bestemt
ved hjælp af 5.853 stk.
MDK fra restaffald og kildesorteret affald.
De følgende kategorier er an-
vendt: mælkekartoner, yoghurtkartoner, flødekartoner, plantemælk- kakao- og vandkartoner, og juice-
og vinkartoner. Analysen viste at der for mælk og yoghurt kartoner er en væsentlig forskel ved at
bestemme typefordeling udefra vægt vs. antallet af kartoner. Forskellene var mere tydelige i affald
fra restaffald end fra det kildesorteret MDK-affald. Mælkekartoner viste den højeste forekomst blandt
MDK både med hensyn til vægt og mængde. Fødevarekartoner blev ikke undersøgt separat på grund
af den store variation i størrelse, volumen og anvendelse.
MDK fra restaffald indeholdt flere madrester end kildesorteret MDK. Forskellige typer kartoner blev
sammenlignet vægtmæssigt fra restaffald og fra husstandssorteret affald. Ud af de 35 sammenlignede
==PAGE:6==
Resume
iv
typer viste otte typer kartoner fra kildesorteret affald en gennemsnitlig vægt, der var mere end 50 %
lavere end den tilsvarende type fra restaffaldet.
Den rene vægt af MDK blev fastlagt ved at veje yderligere 70 MDK, der specifikt blev udvalgt til at
repræsentere kartontyperne med den største forekomst i affaldsprøverne. Ved at bruge den rene vægt
af kartonerne, kunne der bestemmes en gennemsnitlige vægt for de kartontyper der blev fundet mest i
affaldsprøverne. Et vægtet gennemsnit af de forskellige typer blev brugt til at anslå et restindhold for
hver MDK anvendelseskategori og et samlet niveau af restindhold i den indsamlede MDK på 16-21 %.
Der blev gennemført en komparativ LCA for at sammenligne forskellige tilgængelige affaldsbehan-
dlingsmetoder for MDK-affald fra Danmark. LCA’en sammenlignede fire scenarier: forbrænding, fiber-
genvinding i Sverige og Tyskland (henholdsvis Fiskeby- og Hündgen-scenarier) og genanvendelse af
UBC til byggeplader i Sverige (Recoma-scenariet). Den anvendte mængdeopgørelse er delvist baseret
på resultaterne fra denne undersøgelse fra affaldsprøverne og MFA-resultaterne.
Resultaterne viste, at hvert scenarie udmærkede sig i flere miljøindikatorer, hvilket gør det udfordrende
at vurdere det mest miljømæssigt bæredygtige scenarie uden at vægte resultaterne. Med hensyn til
klimaændringer gav genanvendelsesscenarierne miljøbesparelser sammenlignet med forbrænding, som
gav en miljøbelastning.
Generelt gav de forskellige scenarier miljøbesparelser i de fleste miljøindikatorer. Fiskeby- og Hündgen-
scenarierne er ansvarlige for størstedelen af de belastninger, der kan opstå ved UBC-affaldsbehandling,
men også for betydelige besparelser i eutrofiering af ferskvand.
Desuden ligger de normaliserede resultater af de fleste miljøindikatorer for alle scenarier, bortset fra
resultaterne for eutrofiering af ferskvand og økotoksicitet af ferskvand, på en skala fra -0,25 til 0,05
PE. Substitutionen af energi og materialer i scenarierne havde en væsentlig indvirkning på de samlede
resultater, og scenarieanalysen viste, at det anvendte energisystem i modellen i høj grad påvirkede
resultaterne.
På baggrund af undersøgelsen, og inden for de præsenterede usikkerheder ser det ud til at Recoma-
scenariet er den mest miljømæssigt bæredygtige løsning af de vurderede scenarier for forskellige affalds-
behandlingsmetoder.
==PAGE:7==
Preface
This thesis was conducted at the department of Environmental and Resource Engineering at DTU. The
thesis is submitted to fulfill the requirements for the degree of Master of Science in Environmental
Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The thesis accounts for 35 ECTS points and was
written under the supervision of Anders Damgaard and Valentina Bisinella in the time period September
19th, 2022 to April 2nd, 2023.
Prior to the thesis writing period, between August- September 2022, a special course of 5 ECTS was
held to perform data analysis on Econet’s waste analyses regarding food and beverage cartons in Danish
household waste. The report is attached as an appendix to this study.
All the pictures used in this study are taken by the author unless specified otherwise
Kongens Lyngby, April 2, 2023
Yasmina Tolsgaard (s152060)

Acknowledgements
I read somewhere Desmond tutu once said: ”There is only one way to eat an elephant: a bite at a time”.
While I do not intend to encourage eating elephants, or animals in general, it seems like the perfect
metaphor for the thesis writing process.
Writing my thesis has been an exhilarating (and sometimes overwhelming) but also quite a fantastic
process. I have talked and met with so many wonderful people, who have contributed their time, skills,
and expertise to enrich my understanding of the subject, and I’m very thankful to you all.
The following people have made an impact on my thesis, and I owe them thanks:

I would like to especially thank Claus Petersen from Econet for providing some initial data and support
along the way. Econet’s team (where I’m employed) has given me a broader understanding of waste
management and taught me the importance of a ”hands-on” approach to really understand waste
management. These were some invaluable lessons. I would also like to thank Anders Kortegaard and
the sorting team of Econet, who accommodated my ideas for waste sampling and helped me in every
way they could.
I’m very thankful to Malene Møhl from the municipality of Copenhagen, for her insights into the
sorting and recycling processes and for allowing me to use a part of ARC’s sorting plant to conduct
my measurements.
Big thanks are sent to Torben, Jimmy, and Michael from the sorting team for
helping me with my measurement, being kind and welcoming, and contributing with helpful advice and
insights. I would also like to thank Edward Debski from Combineering for the insightful conversations,
for providing data about the sorting and recycling of UBC, and for his insights into the world of
recycling. Thank you, Anne-Sofie Kleemann, from Kreab, for the collaboration and data exchange. I
truly appreciate your dedication and your willingness to help.
I would like to thank Lars Overvad Mathiasen and Mogens Thude from AFLD for their helpfulness and
hospitality. Dedicating a day to show me the facilities in Fasterholt, discussing waste management, and
sharing data and insights were highly appreciated.
I’m very thankful to Rene Jensen from Nordværk. The visit to Nordværk gave me a better understanding
of the theory and taught me a lot about the issues related to sorting and a lot about human error. Thank
you for dedicating the time, showing me around, and explaining everything. I truly enjoyed my visit.
Big thanks are also sent to Max Rosenberg from Recoma for welcoming me to the production hall and
dedicating the time to answer all my questions. I’d also like to thank Michael Brandl for giving me
insight into the German recycling system and being kind and helpful.
On a personal note, I would like to thank Kirstine, Glenn, and Anna for being my support system, both
personally and professionally, throughout the thesis writing period. I could never thank you all enough.
Finally, I would like to thank my supervisors, Anders Damgaard and Valentina Bisinella.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Figure 1.1: Generation of packaging waste in Den-
mark and the EU per capita. The figure was based
on data from Eurostat (2022). Note that the y-axis
starts from 120 kg per capita
The world population is increasing, and prod-
uct consumption is growing along with it. More
and more products are being sold with packag-
ing, which often has a short lifetime, resulting
in ever-increasing amounts of packaging waste
(Pasqualino et al., 2011). Some experts estimate
that global quantities of waste increase annu-
ally by 73% due to urbanization and population
growth (World Bank, 2022).
Packaging is part of product delivery to the
consumer.
According to the European Parlia-
ment and Council directive 94/62/EC (Article
3), ”packaging” is defined as ”all products made
of any materials to be used for the containment,
protection, handling, delivery, and presentation
of goods, from raw materials to processed goods,
from the producer to the user or the consumer.”
(European Union, 1994). The material used for
packaging may be glass, metal, plastic, paper, or cardboard, and have various functions (Jacob et al.,
2021)
The EU’s packaging waste generation rate has increased from 157 to 177 kg per capita between 2011
and 2020. In Denmark, the packaging waste generation rate shows a similar increase to 179 kg per
capita in 2020 (Eurostat, 2022) (Figure 1.1). Of this packaging waste, 41.2% is paper and cardboard,
and 19.5% is estimated to be plastic (Eurostat, 2022). In fact, 40% of plastic and 50% of paper in
the EU are used for packaging. On an EU level, it is estimated that packaging accounts for 36% of
municipal solid waste (European Commission, 2022a).
In Denmark, packaging waste amounts to approximately 780,000 tons or 6% of the generated waste.
The estimated packaging waste originating from households amounts to 376,000 tons or 11% of the
municipal household waste.1
Packaging is necessary for beverages and liquid foods, considering the need to transport the products
from producer to consumer (Boesen et al., 2019). Nevertheless, beverage packaging comes at an envi-
ronmental cost, as the contribution of the materials used for packaging is still debated. According to
Simon et al. (2016), the contribution of packaging is calculated as being up to 45% of the entire food
value chain. Other studies have estimated the contribution of packaging to be considerably lower than
food production (Wohner et al., 2019).
Beverage packaging is a textbook example of the linear model of production and consumption, as
explained by the Ellen MacArthur Foundation (EMF, 2015), where the packaging is manufactured,
sold, used (usually for a short time), and then discarded as waste. Therefore, the role of packaging is
1These are not oﬀicial numbers, but the addition of all packaging fractions in the Waste statistics from 2020
(Miljøstyrelsen, 2022). An assumption is made that half of the paper fraction is used for packaging.
==PAGE:22==
1.1 Objectives
2
under discussion. Packaging can ensure the supply of products and prolong the lifetime of food and
beverages (Boesen et al., 2019), thereby minimizing the environmental impact of the food production
industry by reducing food loss (Wohner et al., 2019). Conversely, due to its relatively short lifetime,
food packaging also burdens the waste management system and society (Boesen et al., 2019).
Beverage cartons (BC, or in Danish: Mad- og drikkekartoner or MDK) are multilayered packages made
of paperboard and thin layers of plastic, and in some cases, an aluminum layer to protect and transport
beverages and liquid foods, either refrigerated or shelf-stable. In 2020, the Danish government added
used beverage cartons (UBC) as a waste fraction to be household collected and shipped to recycling to
increase circular economy and sustainability in Denmark (Danish Government, 2020).
UBCs represent approximately 4% of Danish household waste (Tolsgaard, 2022); however, it is still one
of the nine fractions the Danish government has chosen for special recycling efforts, together with food
waste, glass, plastic, paper, and cardboard, all of which represent much more substantial fractions. But
why?
While most of the existing scientific and life cycle assessment (LCA) literature agrees that BC performs
better environmentally than glass or plastic (in the entire life cycle, cradle-to-grave), it is unclear
whether recycling is better incineration as an end-of-life scenario for UBCs. BC is a low-value product
with a short life span that will never be circular.
Since the new legislation was implemented, a study of the Danish use of BC focused on waste manage-
ment has yet to be conducted. Previous studies are mostly literature-based or product-specific. An
assessment of the recycling of UBCs on a national level (comprised of different products and carton
types) was performed only in the Netherlands (Thoden van Velzen et al., 2017).
This study will evaluate the waste management of used beverage cartons in Denmark using a litera-
ture review, data collection, waste sampling, material flow analysis (MFA), and LCA. It will assess
the potential environmental impact of treatment, comparing the recycling of UBCs with the current
incineration solution for municipal solid waste. The LCA is a comparative assessment and includes
incineration, recycling of cardboard fibers, and recycling UBCs into construction materials.
While there is no question of whether UBCs should be recycled since it is already established by law,
it may be beneficial to understand the different approaches to recycling and evaluate them, as well as
understand what is contained in the fraction sent to sorting and recycling. Since the implementation
of the household collection is relatively new, it will also be beneficial to identify the data gaps in the
current knowledge of the system in order to obtain a better assessment of the environmental impact
related to each of the end-of-life scenarios.
1.1
Objectives
The leading research question in the current study is
Which waste treatment method available for UBCs collected in Denmark is the most environmentally
sustainable?
To answer the research question, the following issues must be considered:
• What is the current state of UBC waste treatment in Denmark since implementing the new waste
strategy (of ten fractions in the household collection)?
• What were the material flows of UBCs in Denmark in 2022?
• Which cartons do Danish consumers use, and which materials do they contain?
• What are the environmental implications of UBC incineration?
• What are the environmental impacts of recycling UBC?
==PAGE:23==
1.2 Structure of the project
3
• What are the data gaps for the current systems, and what is needed for a more comprehensive
understanding of the waste management of UBCs in Denmark?
1.2
Structure of the project
Chapter 2 provides the background necessary to understand beverage cartons and their recycling. Leg-
islative and national requirements are described, including Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR),
the state of UBC recycling in other EU countries, and their recycling initiatives.
Chapter 3 reviews the scientific literature regarding beverage cartons, focusing on waste management
from a Danish- and an international perspective.
Chapter 4 outlines the methodology used in the project to understand national consumption and pat-
terns, including material flow analysis, the distribution of BC types in the waste, and the level of
residues in UBCs.
Chapter 5 illustrates the current state of UBC recycling in Denmark and presents the MFA and waste
sampling results. It also shows the distribution of BC types and level of residues in the UBCs, based on
the data obtained from waste sampling. These results are used further in the LCA part of the project.
Chapter 6 briefly outlines the LCA methodology and includes the goal and scope of the LCA, including
the modelling approaches used.
Chapter 7 presents the Life Cycle Assessment results. It includes the life cycle inventory analysis, the
assumptions made to build an inventory, and the life cycle impact assessment (LCIA).
Chapter 8 consists of the interpretation of the assessment. First, the LCA is evaluated using complete-
ness and consistency checks. The interpretation is also made by using sensitivity analysis: identification
of the significant issues using process contribution analysis and perturbation analysis, followed by data
quality assessment of the parameters evaluated as sensitive. Scenario analysis is performed to test some
of the main assumptions.
Chapter 9 consists of a discussion of the entire study, the results achieved through the different methods,
and the limitations of the results.
Chapter 10 completes the study with conclusions and recommendations.
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2
Background
This chapter will provide the necessary background to understanding the use of beverage cartons in
Denmark. Beverage cartons will be introduced, as well as their recycling processes. The recycling meth-
ods will be outlined, as well as recycling experiences in other EU countries. Finally, waste management
in Denmark and relevant legislation will be presented.
2.1
Beverage cartons
Figure 2.1: Composition of a refriger-
ated carton (CCC, n.d.)
Beverage and liquid food cartons (BCs) are multilayer pack-
ages made of paperboard and thin layers of polyethylene, and
in some cases, an aluminum (Al) layer to protect and transport
beverages and liquid foods. The paperboard lends durability
and rigidness, while the plastic ensures the liquid is sealed
inside and prevents moisture penetration. The optional alu-
minum foil layer is a barrier for oxygen and light (Agamuthu &
Visvanathan, 2014). In this study, BC will refer to liquid pack-
aging boards used in direct contact with food products such as
milk, other dairy products, juice, wine, plant-based milk, and
sauces. Although included in some definitions, pizza boxes
are not a part of the assessed waste fraction in the current
study.
Initially treated with wax, paper has been used in the US as
beverage packaging since 1915, though the use of paper to
carry liquid was first documented back in 1908 (Robertson,
2002) when it replaced glass and metal as packaging for re-
frigerated products only.
Figure 2.2:
Composition of a shelf-stable
(aseptic) carton (CCC, n.d.)
In Europe, the production of milk cartons began in 1932
(Robertson, 2002).
There are two main types of cartons used today, refrig-
erated and shelf-stable.
The refrigerated (gable top)
cartons are made mainly of paperboard and thin layers
of polyethylene. Figure 2.1 shows the composition of
an example of a refrigerated carton.
Shelf-stable (aseptic) cartons have been on the market
since 1969 (Robertson, 2002). These are made for the
ambient environment (Schlecht & Wellenreuther, 2020)
and prolong the contained product’s expiration period.
Often, the products are sterilized and have a shelf life
of over six months (Varžinskas et al., 2012).
According to Varžinskas et al. (2012); Mroziński (2009),
the global market for aseptic packages increases annu-
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Beverage cartons
5
ally by 6-8%. Figure 2.2 shows the composition of an
example of a shelf-stable (aseptic) carton and its layers.
The advantage of multilayer packaging compared to mono-material packaging is that it reduces the cost
of production and consumes fewer materials (Schmidt et al., 2022). Therefore, the transport of the
BC is more eﬀicient, considering fuel and other costs (Khan et al., 2021). Yet, it is perceived as more
diﬀicult to sort and recycle, both by consumers (Boesen et al., 2019) and by the industry (Schmidt et al.,
2022; Robertson, 2021), since the technology used for sorting waste (NIR scanner) can only identify
surface properties and often disregards other layers (Schmidt et al., 2022).
2.1.1
Recycling of Beverage Cartons
UBCs collected in Denmark are sent to sorting plants both in Denmark and abroad and ultimately
recycled abroad.
For more information about the current state of UBC recycling in Denmark, see
Section 5.1. The UBCs undergo mechanical sorting with a NIR scanner to separate UBCs from plastic,
metal, and other impurities. According to Schmidt et al. (2022), NIR scanners can achieve a purity of
up to 96% in the sorting process. See Figure 2.3 for a visualization of the sorting process.
(a) Waste stream of plastic and UBCs
(b) NIR Scanner
Figure 2.3: Mechanical sorting at the test sorting plant in Amager Ressourcecenter (ARC), Copenhagen
Currently, the recycling of BC in Northern Europe is divided into two main options:
• Cardboard recycling- the sorted UBCs are mixed with other types of paper, paperboard, and
cardboard and recycled together. An example of this type of recycling plant is Fiskeby board in
Sweden
• UBC recycling - Sorted UBCs are recycled in plants dedicated to BCs. Three such plants for BC
are located in Germany, where Danish UBCs are also sent.
It is suggested, though, that recycling UBCs in standard mill recycling may not be as eﬀicient as in a
specialized mill, and more of the UBCs will end up as reject and incinerated (ACE, 2022a).
Recycling can be categorized as primary, secondary, tertiary, and quaternary (Robertson, 2021; Henshaw
& Han, 1996). Primary or closed-loop recycling is not relevant in the case of BC since it is impossible
to convert UBC directly into its original use (Robertson, 2021). The long cardboard fibers used for BC
are shortened in the recycling process and cannot be reused for new beverage cartons but may be used
for other carton types.
Secondary recycling, where virgin material properties are not required, and the materials are processed
and used, is the standard recycling method for UBCs (Robertson, 2021). The most common way of
==PAGE:26==
2.1 Beverage cartons
6
recycling UBCs is by separating the paperboard fibers from the PE-Al1 layers. The cardboard fibers
are long and of high quality, making them attractive for recycling (Agamuthu & Visvanathan, 2014).
The separation is done using a hydropulping technology, in which the UBCs are mixed with water in
a concentration range of 3% UBC to 15% UBC by weight, where the remainder is water. The PE-Al
fractions remain in the water and are extracted by pumping (Agamuthu & Visvanathan, 2014).
Recycled paperboard from BC can be used as secondary packaging for food or other products (Mourad
et al., 2008), as well as for corrugated boxes, paper towels, tissues, writing materials, and paper bags
(Geueke et al., 2018).
The remaining fractions of PE-AL and residues are often landfilled (Varžinskas et al., 2012; Meneses
et al., 2012; Pasqualino et al., 2011) but can also be used as construction materials, such as roof
shingles (Varžinskas et al., 2012; Agamuthu & Visvanathan, 2014), incinerated with energy recovery
(Markwardt et al., 2017) or used for heat energy in cement kilns (Wellenreuther et al., 2012; Schlecht
& Wellenreuther, 2020).
An alternative secondary recycling method is shredding the materials and processing them together.
Shredded BC can be used in construction (Robertson, 2021) as a substitute for virgin tree fibers
(Ayrilmis et al., 2008).
This method is less common in the EU. See Figure 2.4 for an example of
converting shredded UBC to construction boards from a new recycling plant in Sweden.
(a) Shredded UBC and input material
(b) Construction boards made at Recoma before
finish
Figure 2.4: Examples of the production process of converting UBC and other input into construction
boards at Recoma in Sweden
Tertiary recycling is also used for UBCs, where the products are broken down into their primary chemical
building blocks (Robertson, 2002).
The recycling of UBC relates to the concept of the circular economy (CE). Instead of the current linear
model, recycling the materials will keep them in the system where they can replace virgin materials
(EMF, 2015) for some functions.
However, closed-loop recycling seems improbable since the virgin
material properties are not available in recycled BC fibers. Plastic layers likewise cannot be reused
for BC due to legal constraints regarding the use of recycled materials in food-contact products (Zero
Waste Europe, 2020).
The Alliance for Beverage Cartons and the Environment (ACE), the European platform for manu-
facturers and suppliers, has published guidelines for design for the recyclability of BC, outlining all
components used by the industry and evaluating their recyclability (ACE, 2022a). Most components
1PE-Al is used as a short-hand term to describe the polyethylene and aluminum fraction of the BC
==PAGE:27==
2.1 Beverage cartons
7
of the BC are compatible with the recycling processes, but some polymers used for lamination, barrier
films, caps, and closures are not (ACE, 2022a).
UBCs have been collected and recycled in many European countries for many years. ACE has proposed
a goal of collecting 90% of UBCs for recycling by 2030 and ensuring that at least 70% of all BCs are
recycled to comply with the EU’s recycling targets (ACE, 2021b).
According to ACE, 51% of the beverage cartons in Europe2, about 450,000 tons were recycled in
2019 (ACE, 2021a). There are approximately 20 recycling plants in the EU dedicated to UBC (ACE,
2021a; Miljøstyrelsen, 2021). The Institute for Energy and Environmental Research in Germany (IFEU)
estimated the European recycling rate to be 30%, with a collection rate of 53% in 2017 (Schlecht &
Wellenreuther, 2020).
An overview of EU countries and their collection and recycling rates is shown in Table 2.1

New EU legislation is set to solve this issue and clarify recycling rate calculation methods (European
Union, 2018). Eunomia Research & Consulting has estimated the national recycling rates for Germany,
Spain, Sweden, and the UK in order to weigh new EU regulations and residues in the UBCs (Zero
Waste Europe, 2020). As seen in Table 2.1, the estimated recycling rates by Eunomia are much lower
than the reported national rates.
In the Netherlands, calculated recycling rates were divided by sorting method. For the Netherlands,
the table shows the results of two waste streams: the PD stream- containing plastic and UBCs, and
the PMD stream- containing plastic, metal, and UBCs (Thoden van Velzen et al., 2017); these are
comparable to the Danish system. It seems as if the collection and recovery rates of UBCs collected
with plastic and metal are higher than those of UBCs with plastic. However, the uncertainties bound
to these values make it impossible to conclude definitively.
For Norway and Finland, recovered PE-Al was also provided by (Markwardt et al., 2017) Yet, the table
indicates only recovered fibers to allow comparability between the countries, since most recycling plants
still dispose of the PE-Al fraction (Markwardt et al., 2017).
Currently, EU countries use different collection schemes for packaging waste, but the main ways to
collect BC are with either lightweight packaging or paper-based packaging (ACE, 2021a).

2.2 Waste management in Denmark and legislation
8
2.2
Waste management in Denmark and legislation
An assessment of end-of-life quotas made for Tetra Pak showed that in 2017, 97.6% of the beverage
cartons in Denmark ended up in an incineration plant, and 2.4% were landfilled (Markwardt et al.,
2017). However, resource strategies towards increasing recycling in Denmark have been on the agenda
for almost ten years (Miljøstyrelsen, 2014).
In 2020, the Danish government presented a new strategy for waste management and circular economy
to reduce national greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions (Danish Government, 2020). The plan included
streamlining municipal waste management, and it was decided that all municipalities should collect 10
different waste streams, with UBC as one of them, beginning in June 2021.
According to the ministerial resolution regarding waste (Affaldbekentgørelsen), municipalities must
ensure an unspecified but high UBC recycling rate (Miljøministeriet, 2021a). The Danish plan followed
the EU targets for recycling and reducing waste generation (Danish Government, 2020).
According to the EU’s strategy plan for circular economy, applying the principles of circular economy can
increase the Gross Domestic Product (GDP) in the EU and create up to 700,000 new jobs (European
Commission, 2020).
In the action plan, goals for packaging waste are presented, such as reducing
packaging and packaging waste and designing packaging materials so that all packaging is reusable or
recyclable by 2030 (European Commission, 2020).
Directive 94/62/EC on packaging and packaging waste has established targets for recycling for 2025
(European Union, 1994). In 2018, the directive was amended as follows:
• By 2026, a minimum of 65% of all the packaging waste (by weight) and 75% of paper and cardboard
(by weight) will be recycled
• By 2031, a minimum of 70% of all the packaging waste (by weight) and 85% of paper and cardboard
(by weight) will be recycled
Moreover, the amendment from 2018 introduced the novelty of establishing recycling rates not based
on the collection rate, but on the weight of packaging waste after sorting that enters the recycling
operations (European Union, 2018).
A new proposal for EU regulation was released in November 2022. It is currently under first reading
and has yet to be accepted. The proposal outlines the measures to reduce waste quantities and GHG
emissions and make existing packaging reusable or recyclable (European Commission, 2022a).
The
council detailed certain types of unsustainable packaging, mainly single-use plastic packaging of different
types3, and proposed prohibiting their use. Beyond measures regarding recyclability, the EU council
also presented a goal of reducing packaging waste generation per capita per member state by 15% by
2040 (European Commission, 2022b).
The proposal for new regulation also suggests making waste
sorting easier by streamlining all waste sorting schemes in the EU (European Commission, 2022b).
2.2.1
Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR)
Extended producer responsibility (EPR) represents a shift in responsibility for packaging waste from
the local governments to the packaging producers. All companies that import or use packaging will
bear the financial and operational responsibility for the waste treatment of said packaging- including
collection, sorting, and treatment (Europen, n.d.).
Currently, many countries in the EU have already established EPR schemes (Europen, n.d.), but by
2024 this will be obligatory, as dictated by the packaging and packaging waste directive (European
Union, 2018, p. 150/144).
3The list of proposed prohibited products can be seen in Annex V of the proposal
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Waste management in Denmark and legislation
9
In Denmark, a Deposit Return Scheme (DRS) was the only existing recycling system for beverage
packaging and only for some types (also known as the pant system) until 2021, while in Germany,
Norway, and Sweden, DRS and EPR have coexisted for years (Bala et al., 2020).
In 2025, EPR will be expanded in the EU to cover all types of packaging. It will also be implemented
in Denmark to ensure effective waste treatment and incentivize producers to modify the design of
packaging to be reusable or recyclable and to reduce the amount of packaging waste (VANA, 2022).
According to the European Commission (2022a), EPR and its fees are an effective instrument to in-
centivize the design and use of more sustainable packaging.
According to ACE (2021a), EPR is a
well-functioning scheme that leads to high recycling rates, as exemplified in Germany and Belgium,
thus proving that DRS is not required for a high recycling rate. On the other hand, Zero Waste Europe
(2020) claims that DRS would be the most effective solution, with proven collection rates of 80-99% for
well-functioning systems. However, this will also require a substantial investment to adapt the current
system to accommodate UBCs.
All types of packaging are included under EPR, and BCs are part of it. It still needs to be determined
how exactly EPR will work in Denmark. Most likely, the packaging waste will still be collected and
sent to treatment by the municipalities, but the producers will provide the financing through collective
schemes (VANA, 2022).
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 3
Literature Review
This chapter will review the literature and research on BC, focusing on the waste management of the
fraction. The review is divided into two sections: Section 3.1 reviews research in a Danish context,
and Section 3.2 reviews international research.
The separation is made because the default waste
management in many countries is landfilling, while in Denmark, incineration is the baseline scenario. A
table summarizing previous LCA studies from Denmark and the rest of the world is shown in Section
3.3, and Section 3.4 summarizes the literature and highlight key messages from the literature review.
3.1
Beverage cartons in a Danish context
The collection of UBC from households in Denmark is still relatively new. However, UBC has been
collected in the municipality of Fredericia since 2010 (Samvirke, 2020), but the decision to collect UBC
for recycling country-wide occurred much more recently, back in 2020. Therefore, the research on this
waste fraction in Denmark is quite limited.
Initially, when the Danish EPA began considering UBC, it was part of a wider waste stream of con-
taminated cardboard, including pizza boxes, UBC, and other similar products. Different organizations
have done individual sorting experiments over the years to estimate potential quantities of the fraction
(Miljøstyrelsen, 2018). The Danish Environmental Protection Agency (EPA, in Danish: Miljøstyrelsen)
has estimated the potential of the new fraction to be 15,000 tons annually (Miljøstyrelsen, 2016) and the
municipality of Fredericia has calculated the financial aspects of collecting and recycling contaminated
cardboard (Affald og Genbrug, 2018). Different collection schemes and the carbon footprint of recycling
were also analyzed by Fredericia municipality (Affald og Genbrug, 2018).
In 2018, Bisinella et al. (2018) assessed different waste management scenarios for beverage packaging in
Denmark. Three waste management scenarios- the return system, separate collection, and incineration
were assessed for different materials, which included composite beverage packaging- modelled as aseptic
cartons. Based on available literature and assumptions, it was concluded that incineration is the prefer-
able treatment for 10 out of 14 assessed impact categories for UBCs (Bisinella et al., 2018, pg. 62). A
literature based LCA, centered on recycling processes of aseptic cartons in Sweden, showed inconclusive
results regarding whether incineration or recycling of all the UBC layers is more sustainable (Sund,
2020). According to Rambøll (2022), recycling is not more beneficial than incineration, considering
climate change (CC), since the assumption is that the recycling processes are driven by fossil fuels, and
virgin production is driven by biomass.
Boesen et al. (2019) compared different LCA results of beverage packaging and Danish consumers’
perceptions regarding the environmental impact of different materials. The consumers’ perception of
packaging was quite contrary to other studies reviewed. Consumers deemed glass bottles to be the
most sustainable and beverage cartons to be unsustainable. The consumers’ perception was on point,
however, estimating BC with caps to be more unsustainable than those without caps (Boesen et al.,
2019).
After the new waste management plan was published, a collection experiment was conducted in Single-
Family (SF) households in Solrød municipality, evaluating the environmental and financial impacts of
collection and recycling. Using Material Flow Analysis (MFA), the report concluded that the collection
==PAGE:31==
3.1 Beverage cartons in a Danish context
11
of plastic and UBC is the best option (Larsen & Steenberg, 2021, pg.
81).
Econet also evaluated
different collection schemes for UBCs (among other recyclables). Econet’s analysis does not definitively
conclude which scheme is best for UBCs, though it seems to lean toward the combined stream of plastic,
metal, and UBC. Furthermore, in the report, experts estimate a UBC collection potential of 65% or 12
kg per household annually (Econet, 2021).
In 2020, an assessment made by the Danish EPA estimated the annual collection of UBC to be about
14,000 tons from Danish households. Recycling this waste had a lower environmental impact in 9 out
of 13 assessed impact categories, CC among them, compared to incineration (Miljøstyrelsen, 2020b). In
December 2022, the projection of potential collection of UBC was updated to 20,000 tons annually by
2025 (Miljøstyrelsen, 2022).
A recent study was conducted in Fyn to compare source segregation with central sorting of Residual
Household Waste (RHW), specifically plastic and UBC (Rambøll, 2022). The analysis concluded that
while the sorting eﬀiciency decreases with central sorting, the overall results favor central sorting since
the quantities are significantly larger in RHW and do not depend on the engagement of the citizens
to source-segregate their waste (Rambøll, 2022).
According to Miljøstyrelsen (2021), there are no
differences between the quality of plastic and UBC collected separately and centrally sorted plastic and
UBC.
IFEU conducted several LCA studies for Tetra Pak, both for the European market (Schlecht & Wellen-
reuther, 2020) and for the Nordic market (Markwardt et al., 2017) of different beverage packaging,
Tetra Pak BCs as well as alternatives such as different plastic (PET and HDPE) bottles, glass bottles,
as well as aluminum and steel cans. According to another study, made by The Swedish environmental
research institute (IVL), the significant life cycle phases regarding Global Warming Potential (GWP)
are transport to retail and waste management (Jelse et al., 2009). Moreover, a substantial fraction of
the GHG emissions from UBCs originates from the incineration of the plastic opening and cap. In Mark-
wardt et al. (2017), assessments were made for each country specifically. It was shown that there are
substantial differences between types of BC in the Danish market; see Figure 3.1 for illustration. The
GWP per 1 L refrigerated BC is in the range of 2.3-42.3 kg CO2 eq. for the cradle-to-grave assessment.
Aseptic cartons range from 43.7-69.3 kg CO2 eq. (all values are taken from Markwardt et al. (2017)).
Moreover, the differences in environmental impacts in favor of BC compared to plastic packaging are
reduced when comparing small packages of 250-330 ml (Markwardt et al., 2017).
Figure 3.1: The GWP of different types of Tetra Pak BC for 1000 ml. The numbers are for the whole
life cycle- cradle-to-grave in Denmark and are given in kg CO2-eq. The values are taken from Tables
91 and 99 (Markwardt et al., 2017)
Note that all the Danish studies in the literature, except for Rambøll (2022), were completed before
the establishment of a collection scheme in Denmark and are based on literature and experiences with
other waste fractions or neighboring countries.
==PAGE:32==
3.2 Beverage cartons in an international context
12
3.2
Beverage cartons in an international context
The GWPs of aseptic and refrigerated milk cartons in Japan were assessed by Yokokawa et al. (2019),
who concluded that by excluding the food content of the BC, the GWP of aseptic cartons is 5%
higher than refrigerated. Moreover, contrary to other studies, the results also showed that the GWP
of smaller-sized BC is almost identical to 1 L BC (Yokokawa et al., 2019), thus contesting the claim
that smaller-sized packages have a higher environmental impact (Wohner et al., 2019; Bio Intelligence
Service, 2010; Meneses et al., 2012). However, it should be noted that Yokokawa et al. (2019) assessed
only GWP.
All three European BC companies have conducted LCA studies to assess the environmental impact of
their products. Tetra Pak compared BC products with other packaging materials (Schlecht & Wellen-
reuther, 2020; Markwardt et al., 2017; Jelse et al., 2009). IFEU conducted a study for SIG Combiblok
comparing two products and concluded that the differences in environmental impact between recycling
and incineration are minor (Wellenreuther et al., 2012). Elopak’s reports were not available to review.
3.2.1
Product LCAs
Many LCA studies of BCs have assessed those with specific uses such as milk, juice, or wine. According
to Meyhoff Fry et al. (2010), recycling was the best end-of-life option for milk cartons, though not
considering GWP, where incineration was the better option. Brock & Williams (2020) concluded that
BCs are the least impactful option for fruit juice and milk, for which the waste management is 25%
recycled, and the remainder partly incinerated or landfilled.
The same conclusion was reached by
Stramarkou et al. (2021), where Tetra Pak packages were compared to PET bottles. Moreover, they
concluded that recycling is better than incineration and landfilling in 6 impact categories out of the
18 assessed: WU, ET, HT, POF and TEc (Stramarkou et al., 2021). UBCs were also found to be less
of a burden to the environment than glass in Banar & Cokaygil (2008). For wine, BC was among the
alternatives with a lower environmental impact (Bio Intelligence Service, 2010). For soy yogurt, it was
estimated that the carbon footprint of BC was lower than that of the PP-cup but that the food loss
was more significant for BC, which led to a higher burden from BC (Silvenius et al., 2011).
In Spain, an LCA focusing on global warming and acidification concluded that for CC, recycling the
cardboard (PE-Al were landfilled) of aseptic BC had a 30% lower environmental impact compared
to landfilling and incineration. However, regarding acidification, incineration was the preferred waste
treatment method (Meneses et al., 2012). The same scenario of cardboard recycling and landfilling
PE-Al resulted in recycling having a lower environmental impact than incineration and landfilling con-
sidering GWP and Cumulative energy Demand (Pasqualino et al., 2011). In Hungary, the recycling
of BC showed by far the best CC environmental performance compared with incineration and landfill-
ing. However, the recyclability of BC was deemed merely average (Simon et al., 2016). In Italy, milk
packaging material: HDPE bottles, and aseptic BC, were compared by Scipioni et al. (2013). Their
study concluded that BC performed better than HDPE bottles in all types of environmental impact
assessed, regardless of the waste management scenario: a combination of waste management methods,
100% recycling, 100% incineration, or 100% landfilling (Scipioni et al., 2013). Incineration with energy
recovery was also deemed more sustainable for milk and juice packaging in Gujba & Azapagic (2011),
compared to landfilling or incineration without energy recovery. Waste management of UBC in China
was investigated by Xie et al. (2013), who concluded that incineration had a lower environmental im-
pact than paper recycling (where the PE and Al are landfilled) for CC and four other (Carcinogens,
Respiratory inorganics, ecotoxicity, and Acidification) out of the 8 impact categories assessed. Never-
theless, separating the plastic and aluminum layers made recycling more environmentally sustainable
than incineration (Xie et al., 2013).
On the other hand, Xie et al. (2011) compared PE pouches and aseptic BC in China for milk. They
concluded that the environmental impact of BC is higher than that of PE, with a modelled waste
management of 15% and 30% recycling for BC and PE, respectively, with the remainder discarded in
==PAGE:33==
3.2 Beverage cartons in an international context
13
a sanitary landfill. The higher environmental impact is primarily due to the raw materials used in the
packaging and the low recycling rate of BC (Xie et al., 2011). Similar tendencies were found in an LCA
conducted in Italy (Bertolini et al., 2016), where the production of packaging material substantially
contributed to the overall environmental impact. The low recycling rate is also mentioned as a reason for
a higher environmental burden for BC. Nevertheless, BC was concluded to have a lower environmental
impact than HDPE and PET in 5 out of 7 assessed impact categories (Bertolini et al., 2016, pg. 542).
In 2013, IVL conducted a study to compare different waste management scenarios for UBC, comparing
incineration and different levels of recycling, both of cardboard fibers and the PE-Al fractions in Sweden
(Hallberg, 2013). Different energy systems were also considered for testing the sensitivity of the results.
They concluded that material recycling saves energy compared to incineration for all analyzed scenarios.
Furthermore, they estimated that recycling performs better than incineration considering GWP when
the renewable energy sources are above 50% (Hallberg, 2013).
3.2.2
Meta-studies of BC
A meta-analysis of LCA studies of BC concluded that the general trend for BC, regarding cradle-to-
gate life cycle, is a lower environmental impact concerning the categories of climate change, cumulative
energy demand, and acidification (Von Falkenstein et al., 2010). ACE published a similar study based
on 17 studies in scientific literature, EPDs, and LCA, reaching an arithmetic mean of 87 ± 40 g CO2
eq. per 1 L beverage. The calculated GWP for BC is considerably lower than for glass bottles, and
the mean and median are also lower than PET. However, with the bound uncertainty, the GWP of the
materials overlap (Wohner & Tacker, 2020).
Sazdovski et al. (2021) conducted a literature review of LCAs regarding beverage packaging and how
the studies relate to the principles of Circular Economy. The recycling of BC was not explicitly assessed,
but the review did mention that most articles did not consider some of the CE principles. Moreover,
it highlighted the importance of modelling the recycling processes in the relevant countries, especially
when the recycling is in a developing country (Sazdovski et al., 2021).
The advantages of BC being recyclable and sustainable packaging, and the findings of the assessments
regarding recycling rates of UBC were questioned by Zero Waste Europe (Zero Waste Europe, 2020),
who contended that the recycling rates shown by the industry were too high and misleading since
they included residues and other impurities and did not comply with the EU regulation for recycling
rates (European Union, 2018), thus making UBC much less sustainable than estimated (Zero Waste
Europe, 2020). The recycling rates are also debated by the Danish EPA since, according to the involved
stakeholders, mixing municipal waste with other streams decreases the traceability of the different waste
materials. It was found that the mixing that takes place during the sorting processes (Miljøstyrelsen,
2020a) makes it diﬀicult to provide exact recycling rates.
3.2.3
UBC Recycling studies
UBC recycling rates were investigated by Mourad et al. (2008), specifically the effect an increase in
recycling rate will have on the milk packaging system in Brazil. They concluded that an increased
recycling rate would reduce the total energy demand and all other assessed impact categories (Mourad
et al., 2008). It should be noted that the gain from recycling here is exceptionally clear in the study due
to the system boundaries that include manufacturing and distribution and the fact that the recycled
fibers are used within the system for distribution boxes.
The recycling processes of UBC were evaluated by Robertson (2021), where different recycling options
were reviewed.
The article suggested that the recycling of UBC should lean towards construction
materials since the theoretical yield in this case is 100%.
The most used recycling processes only
recover cardboard fibers which stand for only 75% of the UBC, and the plastic and aluminum layers
are still a challenge to recycle, both economically and environmentally (Robertson, 2021). The use of
==PAGE:34==
3.2 Beverage cartons in an international context
14
UBC for construction materials, specifically composite panels, was discussed by Ayrilmis et al. (2008),
who showed them as safe for use and having a lower production cost than virgin materials (Ayrilmis
et al., 2008). A study conducted in India investigated the utilization of UBCs to construction boards
(Sujatha et al., 2021), and found wide application for the products in the construction industry and for
furniture. The recyclability of the different materials in beverage cartons is also evaluated by Geueke
et al. (2018) as limited for the cardboard and ineﬀicient separation of the PE-Al layers.
Varžinskas et al. (2012) focused on recycling UBC’s aluminum and plastic layers. They investigated
the carbon footprint of landfilling these layers vs. separating them using plasma technology or using
them for roof shingles, concluding that from both a financial and environmental perspective (regarding
carbon footprint), it was best to recycle the cardboard and press the aluminum and plastic to shingles
(Varžinskas et al., 2012). Demonstrations of separation of the aluminum and plastic layers in small-scale
studies were also discussed by Yan et al. (2015) and Riedewald et al. (2022), both of whom reported
promising results and economic viability. The separation of PE-Al using different methods was also
discussed by Georgiopoulou et al. (2021); Zhang et al. (2009, 2014), and Zawadiak et al. (2017), while
Charlier & Sjöberg (1995) focused only on the recovery of aluminum.
Another recycling option is
to use aseptic cartons to produce cellulose nanocrystals, which are in high demand and have broad
application areas (Akgün et al., 2022). A study in Finland compared different treatment methods for
the PE-Al pulping reject from the UBC recycling process (Khan et al., 2021). They concluded that
chemical recycling (here, pyrolysis) showed the lowest environmental impact compared to incineration
and mechanical recycling (Khan et al., 2021).
As in Bisinella et al. (2018), the concept of a return scheme was also discussed by Abejón et al. (2020).
However, it was deemed technically challenging to find machines that would accept all the suggested
types of packaging, and environmentally, the system had a higher impact than the current central
collection (EPR systems). The establishment of a new parallel collection system has led to a higher
recovery rate of packaging and higher environmental impact due to waste collection and management
(Abejón et al., 2020).
The sorting of UBCs from other lightweight packaging waste was discussed in Feil et al. (2016). Accord-
ing to Miljøstyrelsen (2019), there are no substantial differences in the recycling processes between UBC
sorted with paper and cardboard, or with plastic and metal. The difference lies with the middlemen,
due to the food residues in the UBC, which causes a decrease in the quality of the remaining fraction
(Miljøstyrelsen, 2019). The technical emptiability1 of dairy packaging was investigated in Wohner et al.
(2019). Using LCA, it was concluded that food residue increases the environmental impact, especially
acidification, and terrestrial acidification. For dairy products such as yogurt and cream, residues in-
creased all the assessed impact categories by at least 50% and up to 1800% (Wohner et al., 2019, pg.
15).
3.2.4
General observations
A technical assessment of the collection, sorting, and recycling of UBC in the Netherlands (Thoden van
Velzen et al., 2017) raised an issue with residue and residual waste in the collected waste, which resulted
in the decreased quality of the sorted products. Food residues caused a substantial problem of strong
odor during the pulping process and became a nuisance for the neighbors (Thoden van Velzen et al.,
2017). Food residues were also considered a problem in the reports of Miljøstyrelsen (2021); Wohner
et al. (2019); Miljøstyrelsen (2019), and affected the recycling rates (Zero Waste Europe, 2020, p. 24).
BC production was found to dominate the overall environmental impact of BC (Xie et al., 2011; Bertolini
et al., 2016; Bio Intelligence Service, 2010; Wellenreuther et al., 2012; Scipioni et al., 2013). The caps
of the BC were excluded in several studies (Stramarkou et al., 2021; Banar & Cokaygil, 2008), but were
shown in other studies to contribute more than 30% of the environmental impact (Jelse et al., 2009;
Bertolini et al., 2016). According to Markwardt et al. (2017), the CC load of a carton with a cap is
1The ability to completely empty a package, as defined by Wohner et al. (2019)
==PAGE:35==
3.3 Overview of previous LCA studies
15
between 254% (if bio-based) and 350% higher than the same carton without plastic closure (Markwardt
et al., 2017, p.317-318). In Wellenreuther et al. (2012), however, the environmental effect of the closure
was reported to be minor in most impact categories, with the closure adding approximately 10% to the
assessed impact of the carton.
Consumer behavior is emphasized in some of the studies since it is up to the consumer to sort the UBC
in most cases (Bio Intelligence Service, 2010; Wohner et al., 2019; Yokokawa et al., 2019; Miljøstyrelsen,
2021; Meneses et al., 2012), and it is the consumers’ responsibility to ensure a low level of residue to im-
prove the recycling process, avoid food loss, and lower environmental impact. Other studies highlighted
the waste management of UBC as a crucial part of the assessment (Jelse et al., 2009; Markwardt et al.,
2017; Bio Intelligence Service, 2010; Wellenreuther et al., 2012). Moreover, the environmental impact
of recycling and incineration varies greatly, based on the existing energy alternatives where the study is
conducted. The impact of recycling using coal Xie et al. (2013) will differ from recycling using biomass
or biofuels. It was also shown by Jelse et al. (2009) that the environmental impact of incinerating the
UBC is lower in Denmark than in other Nordic countries since it substitutes a more carbon-intensive
energy production.
3.3
Overview of previous LCA studies
Previous LCA studies regarding beverage cartons detailed in this study are summarized in Table 3.1.
The table should not be used to draw conclusions about recycling vs. incineration and which is the
better waste management scenario. It merely shows the many ways to compare beverage packaging.
However, a great deal depends on the system boundaries and energy- and waste-management systems
in the specific country.
3.4
Key messages from previous studies
Many studies have been conducted on beverage packaging in general and specifically on BC. Since the
product has been on the market for many years, it makes sense that the matter is being investigated.
The studies show that the scenario where the analysis is carried out (i.e., the selected country) has a
relevant impact on the life cycle analysis because it affects the raw material extraction and the packaging
production, transport, and final disposal (Bertolini et al., 2016). Some important notes to be considered
further:
• Most studies focus on aseptic or refrigerated cartons or specific types of beverages, not on national
consumption, which will be a mix of carton types
• LCA studies are case-specific and, therefore, diﬀicult to compare
• The caps of the BC can have a substantial impact on the environmental impact of the product
• Residues can have a massive impact on the product’s environmental impact
• Consumer behavior is an important factor on the overall environmental impact
• The waste management system in the specific country is vital for the overall results

CHAPTER 4
Methodology
This chapter is the first part of the methodology used in the study since the results from the literature
review, data collection, and waste sampling will be used in the Life cycle assessment (See Section 6.1
for the LCA methodology). The literature review, used as a base for the study, is described. The data
collection from Danish municipalities and the different stakeholders is explained. Material flow analysis
is outlined. Lastly, this chapter illustrates the data sampling used in this project to determine the
distribution of BC types and the level of residues in each type.
4.1
Literature review
Several databases were reviewed for the literature survey in this project, though DTU Findit, provided
most of the material. DTU Findit is the online library service of the Technical University of Denmark.
The reviewed literature was found between September 2022 and February 2023. Literature was also
found with the help of the thesis supervisors and their publications. Similar searches were also done
on Web of Science and Scopus. Reference lists from relevant articles such as Brock & Williams (2020);
Bisinella et al. (2018); Wohner et al. (2019) provided additional sources. Lastly, the website Connected
papers was used to find relevant articles.
The literature search included terms such as LCA, Waste, and beverage cartons with variations (e.g.,
aseptic cartons, beverage cartons, tetrapak). Different strings were used to obtain a broader under-
standing of the subject.
Additionally, Econet1 also provided data and previous reports for the literature review. The Danish
EPA (Miljøstyrelsen) reports were also found through other references and used in the current study.
4.2
Data collection
Household collection of UBC is still relatively new in Denmark. Since the municipalities in Denmark
have different sorting schemes, the specific collection scheme for each of the 98 Danish municipalities2
was found in the respective municipal sorting guides. The websites were accessed twice, first in October
2022 at the beginning of the study, and again in February 2023, when some municipalities implemented
new collection schemes.
Eighty-one (81) municipalities were approached for information on their waste system by either mail
or phone.
The remaining 17 did not have a mail address on their website.
Of the missing 17, 15
municipalities were covered by correspondence with their waste companies or the relevant middleman3.
The remaining two have not yet begun the collection of UBC and are irrelevant to this study.
Municipalities that have begun collecting UBC were asked the following:
1A consulting firm working with waste management in Copenhagen
2Excluding Faroe Islands and Greenland
3Middlemen are responsible in many municipalities to take over the recyclable waste fractions after the municipal
collection.
==PAGE:38==
4.3 Material Flow Analysis
18
• Where are the UBCs shipped to?
• What is the sorting eﬀiciency?
• What is the level of engagement of the residents?
• Has there been an increase in collection trips due to the new fraction?
• What is the level of residues in the collected UBCs4?
Update emails were sent out in February 2023 to inquire about the quantities collected in 2022 for a
better estimate of the national UBC collection.
Municipalities that have yet to begin the collection of UBC were asked about their implementation
plans and whether they already have a receiver for the collected UBC.
Next, the waste companies in Denmark were contacted with similar questions, but they were also
asked about shipping and eventual baling of the materials prior to sorting. Middlemen were identified
based on the municipalities’ responses and contacted to inquire about sorting and recycling processes as
well as collected quantities. Sorting and recycling plants were contacted directly and indirectly (using
middlemen) to acquire information for the LCA models.
Contact was established with 66 municipalities, 9 waste companies, 2 sorting plants, and 3 middlemen,
thus covering 96 out of the 98 Danish municipalities5.
The European producers of BC (Tetra Pak, Elopak, and SIG Combibloc) were contacted to establish a
baseline for each carton type or ”clean weight” of the cartons. Important stakeholders in the field were
also identified and contacted. Meetings were conducted with representatives of ”Dansk Affaldsforening”,
”Extr:act”, and ”Affalds- og ressourceindustrien” (ARI). The different stakeholders were contacted to
acquire more data for the models and obtain different inputs for a more meaningful discussion.
Data were also collected in a previous report by the author in August 2022, where Econet’s waste
analyses from 2019-2022 were analyzed for UBC and the size of the fraction in municipal household
waste. UBC was also compared to other fiber products in the RHW, such as paper and cardboard.
Data analysis was performed to estimate quantities and uncertainties to be used in this study. The
report is attached to this study as Appendix J.
4.3
Material Flow Analysis
Material Flow Analysis (MFA) was used to quantify and illustrate what happens to with the post-
consumer BCs once the consumers dispose of them. The MFA system is defined in space and time, and
the law of conservation is applied, so the entire system is balanced using inputs, outputs, flows, and
stocks (Brunner & Rechberger, 2004). In this project, stocks were not used as part of the system since
the main part of the fraction i.e., refrigerated cartons, have a life span shorter than a year, which is
what the model is based on.
The MFA system is built upon the data analysis shown in Appendix J. The starting point of the system
was the amount of generated UBC waste. The model was built with the help of the program STAN 2.6,
which used the starting point, given data, and transfer coeﬀicients to calculate the system as a whole.
The data used for estimating the collection, sorting and recycling of UBCs were provided by different
stakeholders. The literature review was also used to supply estimates and future expectations for the
waste management system. Moreover, literature was also used to compare the calculated values with
expectations and estimates.
4Some of the questions were expected not to be answered since it was assumed that most municipalities had not
worked extensively with the new fraction. Nevertheless, it was deemed worth the chance to ask.
5The remaining two municipalities without data have yet to start a collection of UBC
==PAGE:39==
4.4 Waste sampling
19
The system depicts the state of waste management of UBCs in 2022, where not all municipalities had
begun recycling or had only begun during the last few months of the year. The average collection rate
from either household or waste station collection accounts for municipalities that could not or would
not provide their collection data. Moreover, a range of collection rates is estimated as kg UBC per
household to estimate national collection potential if all municipalities began the UBC collection.
4.4
Waste sampling
Previous Danish studies have shown potential quantities, either from RHW (Miljøstyrelsen, 2018) or by
experimenting with source segregation (Larsen & Steenberg, 2021). According to Bisinella et al. (2017);
Wohner et al. (2019), the waste composition has a substantial impact on the environmental impact
of waste treatment. Therefore, it was deemed essential to conduct and analyze a sampling from the
existing collection schemes. The samples investigated originated from RHW and SS waste streams, as
described further in Sections 4.4.1 and 4.4.2, respectively. The data were registered by hand and typed
into Microsoft Excel, where the results were further analyzed.
4.4.1
Sampling from Residual Household Waste
Econet conducts waste analysis projects for municipalities and agreed to provide materials for the
sampling from two of their current projects, originating in two municipalities on Zealand; see an overview
in Table 4.1.
To simplify the sorting process, the RHW arrived as batches from single or multi-family households in
a non-compacted form. The UBC were coarsely sorted by Econet’s sorting team, then sorted further
by carton type and original content, and weighed individually on a scale of 0-6 kg, with an unknown
measurement error.
4.4.2
Sampling from Source segregated waste stream
The municipality of Copenhagen has an experimental sorting plant within ARC6 that currently sorts
plastic and UBC. The municipality agreed to the use of their materials and plant for the sampling.
UBCs were collected as part of the plastic and UBC fraction in the municipality of Copenhagen and
sent to the transfer station/sorting plant in ARC in a compaction truck. While most plastic- and UBC
waste is not sorted in Denmark but rather baled, packed, and shipped to Germany, the experimental
sorting plant is used for various experiments with waste and currently for plastic waste. The UBC was
separated from the plastic fraction using a NIR scanner. The UBCs were then manually separated based
on original content and type (aseptic or refrigerated). The first few hundreds of UBC were weighed
individually on a kitchen weight, where the assumed uncertainty was 1 g. A bulkier scale of 0-30 kg
± 10 g. was used to make the process more eﬀicient. The cartons were grouped by type and weighed.
Due to the significant uncertainty of the scale, when there were only a few cartons of the same type,
they were weighed individually on the kitchen scale.
There is a known issue with Eddy current separators7 which separates non-ferrous metals but can cause
issues with aseptic cartons due to the aluminum layer. Therefore, the sorted aluminum fraction was also
examined (see Figure 4.3), and the aseptic cartons sorted as metals were also extracted and measured.
Six batches were sampled; see the overview in Table 4.1.
The sampling of UBC was done between November 2022- February 2023, as illustrated in Table 4.1.
The period around Christmas and New Year’s Eve and up to the middle of January was purposely
avoided since it was assumed the use of BC would differ significantly from the rest of the year. Due to
6the local waste treatment station and incineration plant for Copenhagen and the nearby municipalities
7Eddy current separator is one of the machines in the sorting process used to separate non-ferrous metals
==PAGE:40==

making it unsuitable for measuring residues and obtaining a representative weight. It was, therefore,
only used for determining the distribution of BC use categories quantitatively and coarsely.

4.4.3
Determining the distribution of BC types in Denmark
The type of BC used is vital for the model since refrigerated and aseptic cartons have different structures
and contain slightly different materials. The distribution of the different BC types had been analyzed
using past Econet projects in the report in Appendix J. However, the categorization of cartons in the
Econet projects was not based on the type of carton (refrigerated or aseptic), but on content, e.g., a
category of dairy products included both yogurt, cream, and cocoa, where the first two use refrigerated
cartons and the last is sold in aseptic cartons. The division into different BC use categories in this
study was based on Econet’s analyses, but the used categories were slightly modified, as illustrated in
Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Categorization of BC categories, in Econet projects and in this study. This study refined
Econet’s categorization to separate between aseptic and refrigerated cartons, and to more accurately
assess residues’ levels
==PAGE:41==
4.4 Waste sampling
21
The category ”Other dairy products” was replaced by the categories ”Yogurt” (including similar prod-
ucts) and ”Cream”, while the cocoa cartons were placed under an aseptic carton category.
These
cartons were of different types and with very different levels of leftovers, and it was deemed to be more
accurate to separate them in the model. The NIR scanner sorted the cartons at the sorting plant in
ARC. UBCs were then separated manually, based on their original content and type, counted, and
weighted. Pictures illustrating the data sampling can be seen in Figures 4.2, 4.4, and 4.3.
Since some uses of cartons result in more residue (Wohner et al., 2019), the distribution of BC types
based on weight, as done at Econet, can show skewed results. Therefore, the distribution of BC types
is assessed based on quantity and weight and then compared.
4.4.4
Determining the level of residues in UBC
Measuring the levels of residues in the cartons is necessary to obtain an accurate estimate of the
environmental impact of the UBC treatment (Wohner et al., 2019).
A distinction was made for BC with plastic closures, whether the caps were on or off, because the cap
is accounted for in the clean weight of the carton. Furthermore, the presence of the cap is used in
evaluating the impact of the cap on the level of residues left in the UBC.
Two values were necessary to determine the level of residues in the collected UBC: the average weight of
each product after collection and the clean weight (baseline) of each product. First, the average weight
was found using measurements and refined using error calculation. Second, the clean weight was found
using waste sampling and measurements in the lab. This was made on a much smaller scale, 70 UBCs
compared to 5853 UBCs, due to time constraints.
The methodology for error calculation from Barlow (1989) accounts for measurement errors based on
the assumption that the measurements are normally distributed.
For bundles, the average weight of a carton was calculated as follows: x = weight
N
, where N is the
number of cartons in the bundle. Based on the measurement error of σ = 10 g., the standard deviation
per carton is calculated as σi =

For individually weighted cartons, the standard deviation was calculated in excel and was also
assumed to encapsulate measurement error, as seen in the following equation:

==PAGE:42==
4.4 Waste sampling
22
Based on the methodology presented by Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017), the standard deviation was set
to 3% of the UBC weight in cases where only one sample was measured. The average weight calculations
were made for sampling from both waste streams (SS waste and RHW).
A confidence level for the average weight is calculated based on the following key:
For N > 50 samples of the same BC type - the assigned confidence level is high.
For 10 ≤N < 50 samples of the same BC type - the assigned confidence level is medium.
For N < 10 samples of the same BC type - the assigned confidence level is low.
4.4.4.1
Clean weight measurements
The clean weight measurement of the different BC types was deemed necessary to establish a baseline
for each BC type to be able to determine the average level of residues in said type.
Since several producers and many products are on the market, not all products can be measured.
Nevertheless, this type of measurement can give an indication of the level of residues in similar BCs.
The measurements of cartons for clean weight were conducted at the Technical University of Denmark
lab. UBCs were collected, cut in half, washed, and left to dry in a fume hood for approximately 48
hours. It was attempted to measure several samples for each BC type in order to reduce sampling error.
Moreover, BC types with a high confidence level were prioritized in retrieving samples.
The UBCs were weighed before and after the cutting and cleaning process. A precision scale was used
in the lab. Caps were considered and weighed as well, but not straws. The complete overview of the
samples taken can be seen in Appendix I.
There is a measure of uncertainty bound to this method since the calculation is based on a few samples,
between 1-9 for each BC type.
