CHAPTER 5
Results
This chapter will present the results obtained during the data collection and waste sampling.
The
Material flow analysis is built upon the data collected from the different stakeholders as well as the
literature review. The results of the waste sampling are used to determine the distribution of BC types
in the waste, and the level of residues in each type.
5.1
Material Flow Analysis of UBC in Denmark
UBC counts for 4% of the weight of RHW in 36 batches examined from 15 different municipalities
around Denmark. Table 5.1 outlines the slight differences between Single-family (SF) households and
Multi-family (MF) households.
Table 5.1: Overview of the fraction of UBC as found in RGW from Econet’s waste analyses in 15
different municipalities between 2019-2022. SF stands for single-family, and MF stands for multi-family.
The values were obtained from a previous study shown in Appendix J

As shown in Table 5.1, the samples show a slightly lower fraction of UBC in RHW for SF households
in municipalities with UBC collection, while the fraction of UBC remains constant for MF households,
regardless of the existence of UBC collection.
Based on the websites of the 98 Danish municipalities and correspondence with most of the municipal-
ities, as of March 2023, 69 municipalities have implemented household collection of UBC; 25 of them
began collecting during the writing period of the current study. Therefore, information regarding those
municipalities is limited. Figure 5.1 illustrates the current state of UBC collection in Denmark and the
various collection schemes used. For a complete overview and details about the Danish municipalities,
see Appendix H.
==PAGE:45==
5.1 Material Flow Analysis of UBC in Denmark
25
Figure 5.1:
The state of UBC collection in Denmark, as of March 2023, including the different
collection schemes used by the Danish municipalities.
The requirement for household collection of UBC was enacted in 2021. According to Affaldsviden1, in
2021, 36 municipalities collected2 about 280 tons of UBC in Denmark (Affaldsviden, 2022), amounting
to roughly 1% of the national consumption (Extr:act, 2023). In 2022, approximately 50 municipalities
had implemented household collection, and more municipalities began the collection towards the end
of the year. The MFA is based on the waste flows in 2022 and is calculated based on estimates from
previous studies and data from stakeholders. The values used in the MFA are compared with estimates
found in the literature to test their reliability.
The starting point of the material flow system in Figure 5.2 is the Generated UBC waste, which
encapsulates both the BCs placed on the market and the organic residues in the discarded UBCs. The
value of 32,393 ± 4,835 tons per year was found in Appendix J as a weighted average of 15 municipalities
around Denmark and scaled up to be the national potential of UBC waste per year. It can be translated
to 11 kg per household3 in Denmark annually. The literature review gives a broader interval of 24,510-
40,446 tons per year for cartons placed on the market. Estimates for Generated UBC waste are within
this interval. The different estimates and their sources can be seen in Table 5.2.

The estimates for quantities of BC placed on the market are made by the producers. The estimate from
Extr:act (2023) was calculated in collaboration with the three leading European producers of BC in
2021, which makes it especially credible. The estimates for generated UBC waste are based on samples
from different municipalities and expert estimates, and often Econet waste analyses, used as sourced
for the Danish EPA reports. As seen in Table 5.2, the calculated value of the national potential of UBC
waste (marked in green) is within the range of known estimates. Connecting the value of generated
UBC waste to the value of BCs placed on the market, as estimated by Extr:act yields a 20% level of
residues. Considering the uncertainty bound to the UBC waste and assuming the value from Extr:act
is accurate, the level of residues could range between 2-38%.
5.1.1
Collected and Sorted UBCs to recycling
Data regarding the collected quantities of UBCs from Danish municipalities are not publicly available.
All municipalities must report their waste quantities to the Danish EPA, but it was not possible to
obtain the data from the EPA. The municipalities, waste companies, and middlemen were contacted
in February-March 2023 for information on the relevant quantities. Data regarding collected or sorted
quantities were obtained from 7 municipalities, 3 waste companies, and 3 middlemen companies, en-
capsulating the data from all but three of the municipalities that collected UBC in 2022.
Average
collection rates were calculated for municipalities with similar collection schemes (household collection
or collection in waste stations) and used to account for the three municipalities with missing data.
The numbers often used for the collection are the already sorted UBC quantities. For most Danish
municipalities, UBC is collected with plastic or plastic and metal. Therefore, the actual amount of
UBC can only be measured after the sorting process, where some cartons are already lost. The data
from the different stakeholders are proprietary and attached in the confidential appendix. The data
from each source were evaluated for whether it was sorted or collected. The sorted values were adjusted
based on the known sorting eﬀiciency for the relevant sorting plant to establish a collection rate for the
Danish municipalities. For some stakeholders, the data include a mix of collected UBCs from municipal
and commercial use.

and why the uncertainty of the sorted UBC flow is especially high. Since the quantities for the entire
system are fairly uncertain, the uncertainty is reasonable.

5.1.2
Recycled UBC
Different waste streams are mixed in the sorting process, and the traceability of a specific waste stream
is decreased (Miljøstyrelsen, 2020a). It is, therefore, challenging to find actual recycling rates for the
Danish waste streams of UBC. However, there are some estimates of UBC recycling rates. These rates
refer exclusively to the paperboard fibers. The plastic and aluminum fractions are not recycled in all
plants but are incinerated in some for energy. In Germany, some plastic is recycled, but the main plastic
recycling plant for UBC, Palurec, does not have suﬀicient capacity to handle all the plastic quantities
from UBC. Some of the UBC shipped to Germany undergoes tertiary or chemical recycling. According to
DKK plastics (2023), 97% of the UBC is recycled: 70% secondary recycling, and 27% tertiary recycling.
Nevertheless, this MFA will consider only cardboard fiber recycling due to the limited capacity of other
forms for recycling. Based on the literature, the recycling rate is set to be 70.5 ± 1.5%: an average to
account for the recycling rate of 69-72%. Table 5.5 shows the recycling rates from the literature and
obtained data.
Table 5.5: Overview of the different estimates for UBC recycling rates found in the Danish EPA
reports, scientific literature, and involved stakeholders. Marked in green is the value used for Figure 5.2

As seen in Table 5.5, there are different estimates for the loss of materials in the recycling process. Some
of the mentioned recycling rates are for the fibers only, while others include the chemical recycling of
the PE-Al fractions. Several sources agree that about 70% of the UBCs are recycled, mostly cardboard
fibers.
5.1.3
Analysis
The results of the MFA can be seen in Figure 5.2, showing the material flow of BC in Denmark for
2022.
==PAGE:49==
5.1 Material Flow Analysis of UBC in Denmark
29
Figure 5.2: The material flow of beverage cartons in Denmark in 2022. The numbers are given in
tons. The green arrow indicates the recovered cardboard fibers as secondary materials
The figure shows that most of the UBC waste in 2022 was incinerated, and there is a long way to go to
achieve the collection rate of 20,000 tons annually, as established by the Danish EPA (Miljøstyrelsen,
2022). From the values used here, it can be calculated that 20% of residues and impurities are added to
the BC in the post-consumer stage. This level of residues fits quite well with findings from Thoden van
Velzen et al. (2017) from the Netherlands. The current national collection rate of UBC is approximately
5% of, and the recycling rate is 2.5% of the generated UBC waste, but the recycling rate is highly
uncertain. The collection rate is based on data from most involved stakeholders and is assumed to be
reliable. Though it should be said that not all Danish citizens as yet have the technical possibility to
sort at home or at all, which could contribute to the low collection and recycling rates. Moreover, UBC
as a fraction is still relatively new in Denmark.
It was not possible to calculate a municipal collection rate for all the municipalities since some of the
data were obtained through middlemen, which is a summary of many municipalities; some of them only
started collecting UBC in the middle or towards the end of 2022, which will make a collection rate
diﬀicult to estimate. The collection rates in the different municipalities vary between 0.11 and 5.78 kg
per household annually, averaging 0.89 kg per year per household (shown in Table 5.3). The potential
national collection is 2,615 tons annually based on the average collection rate in 2022, but if all Danish
municipalities had the same collection rates, it could be between 371 and 17,018 tons annually. The
range of the potential collection is very broad, but there are many aspects that affect the collection
rate: socio-economic conditions, technical conditions, municipal nudging, engagement, environmental
awareness, and more. Moreover, for many municipalities, the collection of UBC is a new scheme, and
it is expected to mature and improve with time.

5.2 Determining the Distribution of BC types
30
5.2
Determining the Distribution of BC types
The distribution of the different UBC types was determined for all UBC samples from RHW and SS
waste. The distribution of BC types was determined twice, using the quantities and weight of the BCs
separately, due to residues and their potential effect on the results. Figure 5.3 illustrates the differences
between the two measurement methods for UBC samples from SS waste.
Figure 5.3:
The distribution of UBC types, from the SS waste sampling from Copenhagen.
The
distributions are given in percentage based on quantity and weight. Based on data shown in Appendix I
Figure 5.3 illustrates that more milk cartons are in the sample, but they are underrepresented regard-
ing weight. The same tendency was seen by Miljøstyrelsen (2018), where milk cartons accounted for
71% of the measured quantity but only 59% of the weight. On the other hand, Yogurt cartons are
overrepresented regarding weight due to their high level of residues. The differences between quantity
and weight are less noticeable in the other carton categories.

comparison of the results from RHW and SS waste samples in total.

5.2 Determining the Distribution of BC types

SS waste batches, the first two differed substantially from the remaining four, with more yogurt cartons
and fewer plant-based milk cartons. In one of the batches, it was assumed that a coffee shop had used
the municipal solution to dispose of their waste since the cartons were of a more homogeneous nature,
with previously unseen products5, nicely folded, and with minimal residues.
The over-representation of yogurt discussed for SS UBC is even more pronounced when examining RHW
waste, where yogurt cartons account for 34% of the total weight but only 11% of the total quantity.
The increased presence of plant-based milk explains differences in the category ”Cocoa, plant-based
milk, and water” between SS and RHW waste. It seems that more plant-based milk is consumed in
Copenhagen than in other Danish municipalities. It is also interesting that food cartons have a greater
presence in RHW. The examined batches from RHW showed that food cartons represent 4-28% of
the total quantity of UBC in RHW but only 5-14% of the total weight. For comparison, in SS waste
sampling, food cartons represented 2-5% of the total quantity of UBC and 2-4% of the total weight.
5.2.1
Food cartons
Food cartons were found in the different batches, but as a small fraction of the total amount of UBC
sampled, in total, 152 cartons or 3% of the UBC in the SS waste stream. They were, therefore, not
divided further into volume and content, though they were all aseptic. Baby formula was also considered
a food carton. The wide variety and low occurrence of each type of carton, as illustrated in Figure 5.4,
made it impossible to reach a meaningful and certain conclusion for this carton type.

5.2.2
Agglomerates
During the data sampling, agglomerates6 were found to be part of the batches. Some agglomerates
were clearly the result of consumer behavior, while others could have occurred in the compaction truck.
Examples of agglomerates are shown in Figure 5.5.
(a) Milk carton filled with foils
(b) Cream carton in a cheese container
Figure 5.5: Agglomerates found in the UBC waste stream at the sorting plant in ARC, Copenhagen
Agglomerates were also found and mentioned in Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017) and observed in a
tour of the sorting plant Nordværk. These occurrences in the sorted UBC make it clear that the NIR
scanner can capture some of those cases, though it is unknown how many agglomerates were in other
plastic fractions or as reject.
5.2.3
Comparison of the distribution of UBC types with other studies
Table 5.7 shows the results obtained through waste sampling compared with similar studies from dif-
ferent sources. Some were collected as part of this study (marked as own work). The data from other
Danish municipalities originated from Econet projects, and the results are taken from Appendix J. The
data from Econet’s projects are not entirely comparable due to the change in type categorization, as
explained in Section 4.4.3.
It would have been ideal had the results from the Econet projects been compatible to use, given that
they are from 11 municipalities and based on 27 samples, therefore giving a better representation of
the national consumption of BC. However, those are based only on the weight of UBC in each category
rather than quantity, which makes them unsuitable for use since the levels of residues skew the results,
as shown in Figure 5.3. Instead, the results from Copenhagen municipality (marked in green), which
were obtained as part of this study, will be used further in this project since they are deemed the most
suitable to assess UBC collection and recycling in Denmark.
The data from the collection experiment in Solrød municipality (Larsen & Steenberg, 2021) was recal-
culated to be comparable with the assessed systems, as shown in Appendix I. The data shown are only
from their experiments for a combined waste stream with UBC and plastic. They are not used in this
project since they were perceived as unreliable. The minimal number of households participating (22
6The term agglomerate is taken from Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017) to describe a collection of UBC and other
materials in the waste pasted together

It is beneficial to compare the results since the distribution of BC types from sorted wastes could be
skewed as they are only showing what people chose to sort and not their actual consumption of BC.
Often people tend only to sort some types. From the sampling, it is clear that milk cartons are more

5.3 Residues in the UBC
35
5.3
Residues in the UBC
It was crucial to determine the level of residues in UBC since that affects the environmental impact of
the packaging and the recycling processes in the life cycle assessment. Residues were analyzed in two
ways:
First, by comparing the level of residues in the two investigated waste streams, SS waste, and RHW.
Second, the clean weight of the carton was found using measurements in the lab to establish a baseline
for each carton type and to determine the average level of residues in each type and for each BC category.
5.3.1
Comparison of the level of residues in different waste streams
Not all products were found in both RHW and SS waste streams; 35 types were found in both waste
streams and are compared based on their average weight in Table 5.8. The confidence level assigned is
based on the key given in Section 4.4.4.
Three of the 35 sampled BC types show a higher average weight in SS waste than RHW. However,
when considering standard deviation, only two products are heavier (i.e. contain more residues) in the
samples SS waste (marked in red in Table 5.8). Interestingly, the carton types whose average weight is
higher in SS waste, as compared to RHW, are of small volumes, between 150-250 ml, but these average
weights have low confidence levels, so the data are too uncertain to make conclusions.
Food cartons are marked with a low confidence level, regardless of the number of cartons weighted, due
to the wide variations in size and volume.
The UBCs from SS waste generally show a lower weight than the UBCs from RHW for the remainder
of the sampled cartons. Eight of the assessed UBC types from SS waste show a weight that is less
than 50% of the average weight for the same UBC type in RHW (marked in green in Table 5.8. The
substantial differences between the two waste streams can be explained by the fact that sometimes
consumers discard filled BC if they are past the expiration date or for other reasons. For example, the
average weight of 1 L yogurt BC from RHW is 289 ± 157 g., much higher than the clean weight of such
a carton, which is estimated to be 32 g. The same can be seen for Cocoa 1 L w/ cap, whose average
weight from RHW is 125 ± 125, while the clean weight of the carton is estimated to be 32.3 g. See the
complete list of the compared products and their average weights in Appendix I.

assigned ”High”. 10 ≤N < 50 is assigned ”Medium”, and N < 10 is assigned ”Low”. Marked with

5.3 Residues in the UBC
37
5.3.2
Residues in the UBC based on clean weight measurements
Due to technical diﬀiculties, the UBCs analyzed at ARC and Econet’s sorting hall could not be used for
the clean weight measurements. The BCs producers were contacted regarding the clean weight of their
cartons, but the information was held due to propriety reasons. Therefore, a different type of sampling
was conducted.
70 UBCs were collected for clean weight measurement based on the cartons with the highest occurrences
in the waste sampling. The cartons were collected from personal use, friends, neighbors, and the local
plastic and UBC waste containers. The cartons were weighed on the precision scale mentioned in Section
4.4.4.1 before and after the cleaning and drying.
Some bias is assumed in the measurements of residues before the cleaning process since the cartons
were not randomly selected but collected by people specifically for this study. Some people washed
their cartons before the collection, and it can be assumed that they were generally more aware of
residues in the cartons than in average household collections.
Therefore, the level of residues was
determined based on the average weight obtained from waste sampling in ARC and the clean weight
measurements (the results obtained after the cleaning) from the lab.
Food cartons were not part of the analysis to determine the level of residues in specific carton types. As
described in Section 5.2.1, food cartons were measured in batches in this study due to the wide variety
of types and volumes. It was attempted to have approximately three samples for each type to reduce
sampling error, but diﬀiculties in finding samples caused some carton types, such as cream and soy
yogurt, to be less certain. In each of the categories, there were carton types that were not measured due
to the limited scope of the clean weight measurements. However, cartons were found (and purchased
to some extent) to make the results for each category representative of at least 85% of the carton types
in the category. The results can be seen in Table 5.9.
The table illustrates that the level of residues varies substantially, depending on the carton type and the
category. Milk cartons show the lowest level of residues on average, with juice and wine following. The
lower levels of residues compared to yogurt and cream may be due to the beverages’ viscosity, which
increases the emptiability of the cartons, as Wohner & Tacker (2020) suggested. Cartons with a plastic
opening but without the cap show a consistently lower level of residues than those with the cap on.
Unsurprisingly, the level of residues in cartons without a plastic cap is lower than in the same type
with a cap. This may be explained by the fact that collection trucks use compactors to collect waste
more eﬀiciently, so some of the residues are lost during the collection process and accumulate in the
collection truck.

The overall level of residues found to be 16-21% fits well with the existing literature. According to
Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017), the average level of residues in the Netherlands was estimated at
25%. Another estimate was made by Zero Waste Europe (2020) that maintained that 17% of the waste
consists of moisture and contamination.
5.4
Summary of the obtained results
This chapter presented the results of the MFA and waste sampling.
As of March 2023, 69 of the Danish municipalities had begun a household collection of UBC, and 13
more collected UBC at waste stations. The MFA shows a current collection rate of approximately 5% of
the generated UBC waste, and a recycling rate of approximately 2.5%, though this is highly uncertain.
The waste sampling was made for two waste streams: RHW and SS waste. The results illustrate that
the distribution of UBC type varies substantially for milk and yogurt cartons when considering quantity
and weight. The difference between quantity and weight is more noticeable for UBCs from RHW. The
results also showed that milk cartons are the most common cartons, regardless of waste stream or
method. Since the weight of the residues may skew the results, it was decided to use the distribution
based on quantities from SS waste further in the study. Differences between the quantity and weight of
refrigerated vs. aseptic cartons were not as notable. The results were compared with previous studies.
The UBC’s residue level was determined for cartons from RHW and SS waste streams and compared.
Generally, the UBC from RHW contained more residues than those from SS waste. Out of the 35 BC
types compared, eight types from SS waste showed an average weight lower by more than 50% compared
to the equivalent type from RHW.
Clean weight measurements were conducted for selected BC types to establish the baseline weight of
the different carton types. The baseline was used to calculate the level of residue for the different carton
types and a weighted average for each use category. The overall level of residues is set as 16-21%, as
shown in Table 5.10.
==PAGE:60==
CHAPTER 6
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA)
This chapter will outline the LCA methodology and how it can be used to compare waste management
scenarios. It will also go through the goal and scope stages required by the ISO standards 14044 and
14044 (ISO, 2006a,b). The results in form of inventory analysis and impact assessment are shown in
Chapter 7, and the interpretation is covered in Chapter 8.
6.1
LCA methodology
LCA is a standardized (ISO, 2006a,b) assessment methodology to quantitatively assess the potential
impacts of products and systems. It is intended to use all life cycle phases to avoid burden shifting
from one life cycle phase to another (e.g., a product can be improved to prolong its use phase, but that
may negatively affect the waste treatment of the product by making it diﬀicult or hazardous to recycle)
(Hauschild et al., 2018). While LCA can consider a system’s social and economic aspects, the focus
is typically on the environmental aspect. The assessment is used to calculate potential environmental
impacts and tackle hot spots in different life cycle phases.
The environmental LCA covers a wide
range of environmental indicators to account for different parts of the environment, again to avoid
burden shifting from one issue to another. These indicators include freshwater use, land occupation
and transformation, climate change, resource depletion, and eco-toxic effects (Hauschild et al., 2018).
Various methodologies exist to assess the different issues, covering different impact categories.
LCA is an iterative assessment comprised of 4 main parts, as illustrated in Figure 6.1. The requirements
for the LCA will be incorporated in this report.
Figure 6.1: The four parts of the life cycle assessment framework. The arrows visualize the intercon-
nected and iterative nature of the assessment (ISO, 2006a). Each part is provided with the chapter or
section number where the part is presented in this study
==PAGE:61==
6.2 Goal
41
The main limitation of a life cycle assessment is that it is very specific. An LCA cannot give a generalized
answer of which system is environmentally sustainable. A comparative study can determine which of
the assessed systems is more sustainable within specific impact categories, which represent different
environmental issues. However, the results are valid only for the assessed systems with those specific
assumptions and system boundaries used. Other assumptions or boundaries for the assessed systems
may yield different conclusions. Moreover, vast differences can arise from system boundaries, modelling
choices, and various assumptions made in the modelling process, making it diﬀicult to compare different
LCA studies (Brock & Williams, 2020; Terlouw et al., 2021).
The following sections are based on the LCA Handbook and the requirements for each part, as outlined
in Hauschild et al. (2018).
Section 6.2 and 6.3 will present the goal and scope of the LCA study,
respectively. Section 7.1 will present the key data used to model each of the assessed scenarios, with a
more detailed description of the life cycle stages and modeling choices in Appendix B, and a confidential
appendix for the recycling scenarios. Based on the previous sections, a life cycle impact assessment
(LCIA) will be conducted, and the results will be presented in Section 7.2. The interpretation of the
LCA, including sensitivity analysis, will be shown in Chapter 8. The limitations are incorporated in
the discussion in Chapter 9, and the conclusions of the LCA are shown as part of the entire study’s
conclusion in Chapter 10.
6.2
Goal
This assessment aims to provide the potential life cycle environmental impacts of four different European
waste treatment systems for used beverage cartons (UBC) collected in Denmark. The assessed scenarios
are
• INC- incineration of the UBC along with the remaining residual household waste. This is used as
the baseline or business-as-usual (BAU) scenario.
• Fiskeby- collection and sorting of the UBC in Denmark and recycling of the cardboard fibers in
Sweden
• Hündgen- collection of the UBC in Denmark, baling and packing the waste in transfer stations in
Denmark, shipping the waste to sorting plants, and then recycling plants for the cardboard fibers
in Germany
• Recoma- collection and sorting of the UBC in Denmark and recycling the UBC into construction
boards in Sweden
The first three scenarios are based on the actual utilization of collected UBCs in Denmark. The fourth
scenario of converting UBCs into construction boards is a new and less common recycling opportu-
nity. The findings should identify which of the assessed treatment systems is preferable based on the
performance in different environmental impact categories.
This comparative assessment should be used as an appraisal between the investigated systems. The
results cannot be used to generally determine which waste treatment system is the most environmen-
tally friendly, but only for the investigated conditions and within the system boundaries. Thus, this
assessment does not aim to assess the overall use of beverage cartons. It is an end-of-life LCA, meaning
that the assessment starts with the disposal of UBC into waste containers and focuses on evaluating the
beverage cartons after the use phase. The waste generation occurs in Denmark; therefore, only treat-
ment plants within close vicinity, in Sweden and Germany, are evaluated. While specific municipalities
ship their UBC to recycling plants elsewhere, those systems were not assessed. Tertiary recycling and
recycling of the plastic and aluminum fractions will not be assessed due to the limited use of these
processes and the lack of relevant data. Moreover, due to data constraints, the incineration process
is modelled for municipal solid waste as bulk and not specifically for UBCs, so some process-specific
==PAGE:62==
6.3 Scope
42
emissions may be overrepresented. Lastly, waste sampling is done for the municipality of Copenhagen.
Some geographical variations in the use of BC can be expected.
The purpose of this assessment is to quantify the potential environmental impact of different UBC
waste treatment options and then to indicate a preferred system. It was already decided that all the
municipalities in Denmark should collect UBC from households as part of the government plan for a
greener waste sector and circular economy (Danish Government, 2020). Since the implementation of
the household collection of UBC and recycling is still in an early stage, a lot can change in the years to
come. Therefore, based on the ILCD guidelines, the decision context is Situation B ”Meso/macro-level
decision support (European Commission, 2010).
The target audience is peers and professionals within the fields of environmental engineering and LCA
practitioners of solid waste management systems, as well as other interested stakeholders.
This assessment is not commissioned by any interested party and is made as part of a master’s the-
sis. While it is conducted based on the ISO 14044 standard, this LCA does not live up to the ISO
requirements since it is not critically reviewed by LCA professionals.
6.3
Scope
This study is a comparative study of four different waste treatment systems. The study includes a
detailed Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) for all the processes used and a Life Cycle Impact Assessment
(LCIA), including characterized and normalized results. The results will be analyzed using sensitivity
analysis to determine their robustness and to test the main assumptions in the models.
6.3.1
Object of the assessment
The functional unit (FU) of this study is as follows:
”The treatment of used beverage cartons collected from households in Denmark in 2025. Waste manage-
ment occurs partly in Denmark and partly in other countries in Northern Europe.”
The LCA assessed the environmental impact related to each of the assessed waste treatment options.
It is assumed that recycled cardboard fibers compete only with virgin or recycled material of the same
type since the substitution is based on functionality. As for the construction boards from recycled UBC,
it is assumed that it replaces virgin materials used for the same purpose as the recovered materials. The
scenarios are described in more detail in Section 6.3.2.
The reference flow chosen for this assessment is:
”One ton of used beverage cartons”
The used beverage cartons include both the packaging materials for the cartons and residues of their
content.
The ratio of the two different carton types, aseptic and refrigerated, the ratio of the use
categories, and the corresponding level of residues are based on the waste sampling results presented in
Sections 5.2 and 5.3, respectively. Table 6.1 shows the reference flow used.
==PAGE:63==
6.3 Scope
43
Table 6.1: The reference flow for UBC collected in Denmark
Carton type
Fraction
Composition

6.3.2
System Boundaries
This LCA is an end-of-life assessment, meaning that it deals with the beverage cartons post-consumer,
i.e.
after the products were disposed of in the waste container.
The evaluation is based on waste
sampling from 2022-2023 and other data sources available in 2023. However, comparing the different
waste treatment systems is set in the future, starting from 2025, when the household collection of UBC
will be completed.
Moreover, the reference flow is based on waste analyses from source-segregated
municipal solid waste, which means that some types of UBC may be underrepresented due to sorting
patterns of the residents of Copenhagen or their use as on-the-go products, which ends up in a different
waste system. Littering of UBCs is estimated to be 0.2% by Abejón et al. (2020) and is therefore
disregarded in this study. Nevertheless, the assessment disregarded any eventual behavioral changes to
the consumption and use of the different BC types.
The assessed systems include the collection of UBC, the use of transfer stations if the sorting occurs
abroad, sorting of the fraction, treatment of the waste, and management of the treatment residues.
The emissions to air, water, and soil due to the waste treatment were included within the system’s
boundaries. Moreover, the assessment accounted for the avoided emissions by the recovery of energy,
heat, and materials from the waste treatment. For further details on the modelling of recycling processes,
see Section 7.1.1.
The assessed waste management systems were set to occur in Denmark and Northern European countries.
The waste collection, baling, and packing into bales are set only in Denmark. Sorting of UBC occurs
either in Denmark or Germany, while the recycling processes and management of the treatment residues
were set to occur in Germany or Sweden.
Excluded from the system boundaries are the construction and maintenance of capital goods used for
waste management, such as facilities for sorting and recycling, and the construction and maintenance of
the machinery and vehicles used in the models. The exclusion of these processes is justified due to the
marginal effect the reference flow will have on the capacity of the assessed plants. A cut-off percentage
was not defined in advance.
==PAGE:64==
6.3 Scope
44
The system boundaries for the incineration model, and the avoided processes, can be seen in Figure
6.2. The system depicted in the figure is used to model the incineration process in Denmark in all the
assessed scenarios.
Figure 6.2: System boundaries for the incineration model, including avoided processes for energy and
materials. This is the system for incineration in Denmark used in all assessed scenarios
As shown in Figure 6.2, the assessed system for incineration begins with the collection of UBC in the
households. For an illustration of the full life cycle of BC, see Section A. The background processes in
the model include energy recovery and material recovery from the residues of the incineration process.
The incineration is considered a baseline in this study due to the results of the MFA shown in Section 5.1.
The UBC will be collected from the households and transported to an incineration plant in Denmark
as part of the municipal solid waste. The UBC will be incinerated, and electricity and heat will be
recovered from the process, with 25.4% and 72% eﬀiciency, respectively, based on Bisinella et al. (2021).
The residues from the incineration process are used as follows: The bottom ash was used in Denmark
for road construction, substituting gravel. Aluminum residues were assumed to be treated in Denmark.
Fly ash was recycled in Europe.
==PAGE:65==
6.3 Scope
45
(a) Recycling scenarios in Sweden- Fiskeby and Recoma
(b) Recycling scenario in Germany- Hündgen
Figure 6.3: System boundaries for the assessed recycling scnearios
Figure 6.3 illustrates the different recycling models assessed in this LCA. Figure 6.3 a) is the recycling
of UBC in Sweden- where the system boundaries are identical for both forms of recycling: recycling of
only the cardboard fibers and recycling of UBCs in their entirety to construction boards.
The UBC will be sorted in Denmark with 90% sorting eﬀiciency. Loss of residues totaling 8% will occur
during transport and baling. The UBC will then be baled and sent to recycling in Sweden.
In the first scenario, modelled after the recycling plant Fiskeby board, the fraction is mixed with all
other types of cardboard, and the cardboard fibers are recovered using hydropulping. The PE-Al layers
are treated as reject with other impurities and incinerated with energy recovery.
system a) also depicts the scenario of recycling UBC into construction boards modelled after the pro-
duction company Recoma AB. Here, the UBCs are shredded with PE-Al rejects from other recycling
plants and previous production residues, heated and molded together, and made into the filling for
the construction board. The boards are then coated by recycled paperboard and LDPE and can be
used directly in construction. In order to keep within the system boundaries, the UBCs replace virgin
pulpwood and plastic as filling for a board, illustrating the savings of making such boards with virgin
materials.
The recycling processes in Recoma do not lead to the incineration of residues since the residues from
the treatment are re-fed into the system and used for new boards.
Figure 6.3 b) illustrates a similar scenario but with the addition of a transfer station before the sorting
processes, which is how UBCs sent to Germany for sorting and recycling are treated. The UBC, usually
combined with plastic and metal, will be collected and sent to a local transfer station. The waste will be
baled, packed, and sent to the sorting plant there. The UBC will be sorted from other waste fractions,
baled again, and sent to a dedicated UBC recycling plant in Germany. The cardboard fibers will be
recovered using hydropulping. Due to data constraints, the recycling in Germany is modelled after the
==PAGE:66==
6.3 Scope
46
recycling plant Fiskeby board in Sweden, but with German boundary conditions, i.e., the energy use
and incineration processes are modified to reflect the German market. Some of the PE-Al in Germany
is sent to the recycling plant Palurec where the materials are recovered. This will not be modelled in
this LCA due to a lack of data; therefore, the reject is modelled to be incinerated.
6.3.3
Modelling approach and handling of multi-functionality
The modelling approach used for this assessment is consequential LCA. This approach is the most suit-
able since the assessment is under decision context B and may be used for decision support. Moreover,
the LCA is modelled to take place in 2025, when household collection of UBC will be implemented in
all 98 Danish municipalities.
Multi-functional processes, or processes that provide more than one function in the system, were treated
using system expansion or credit system (Hauschild et al., 2018), which means that additional functions
of the processes, such as secondary materials from recycling or recovered energy and heat from inciner-
ation, were added to the system to displace products in the market. The assessed system gets credit
for avoided primary production.
The consequential approach aims to assess the changes to the economy caused by introducing the studied
system (Hauschild et al., 2018). The displaced products, also referred to as ”marginal technologies” or
”market processes,” are the products that will likely react to changes in demand/supply induced by the
assessed scenarios (Bisinella et al., 2018). The consequential approach also enables assessment of the
extent to which virgin production is avoided through the recycling of the product after use, and the
system is expanded to include these avoided processes (Ekvall et al., 2020).
In light of the uncertainty involved in building future models and predicting the energy mix for Denmark,
Sweden, and Germany, different mixes were assessed, as recommended by Hauschild et al. (2018), to
analyze how sensitive the results were for the energy mix. For further details on the energy mix used
for the different countries, see Appendix F.4
Microsoft Excel was used for the different calculations of the assessed processes, such as the reference
flow. The LCA was carried out with waste LCA software EASETECH 3.4.0 (Clavreul et al., 2014).
EASETECH contains a library of material fractions and waste treatment processes, which are used
for establishing the reference flow and management of residues from treatment. Some processes were
modified to accommodate newer processes using the database Ecoinvent 3.7.1 (Wernet et al., 2016).
Moreover, Ecoinvent was used to model the recycling processes and avoided products using the con-
sequential approach.
For a complete list of the processes used in the different waste management
systems, see Appendix B. The data regarding the recycling processes are of proprietary nature and will
be attached as a confidential appendix for the supervisors and censor only.
The LCIA methodology used was the Environmental Footprint 3.0 with long-term impact with toxicity.
This methodology was used since it was the most up-to-date methodology in the EASETECH software
and since Europe represented its geographical scope (European Commission, n.d.).
Table 6.2 shows the 16 different impact categories used in the assessment along with their abbrevia-
tions which will be used later in the assessment, and the indicators used to measure the impact of
each category. The impact assessment in Section 7.2 will include characterized and normalized results.
The characterized results are given in specific units per impact category, as shown in the table. The
normalized results are based on the European Person Equivalent (PE). The PE describes the impact
associated with the average European resident from various activities, which could be helpful to set the
models’ results in context (Sala et al., 2017). The normalization factors can be seen in Table 6.2. The
indicators used for measuring each impact category and references can be seen in Appendix C.

6.3.4
Representativeness of the LCI data
Technological representative- The technologies used for incineration and recycling should represent cur-
rent technologies used to treat UBC. The incineration scenario is based on data from ARC obtained in
2020 by Bisinella et al. (2021). An LCA of beverage packaging from 2018 was used for missing data
and processes (Bisinella et al., 2018), especially the management of residues from treatment.
The sorting plant in Denmark is modelled after Nordværk, which is presumably not representative of the
technology in the future. Due to government requirements, the plant cannot invest in new technology
or expand its current system since it is owned by municipalities and must be sold soon. Therefore, part
of the sorting is made manually. The increased manual labor is expected to be a short-term condition
and will be phased out when the plant is sold. The technology used to convert BC into a construction
board is about two years old, but the process is still under development and does not operate with
maximal eﬀiciency.
The recycling of UBC in Germany is modelled after the recycling plant in Sweden.
The German
recycling plants are different since they are dedicated only to UBC, while the Swedish recycling plant
uses paper and cardboard, where UBC is part of the input. This is a substantial difference, but the lack
of data regarding the recycling processes in Germany made it necessary to give an estimate, especially
since most of the Danish UBC waste is treated in Germany.
Temporal representativeness- the comparative LCA is modelled to take place in 2025 when all the
municipalities have implemented the obligatory household collection of UBC, and the collection of
UBCs is not a novelty anymore. The LCI inventories are based on recent data assumed reliable.
Much of the data for the recycling scenarios were collected specifically for this study in 2022-2023. The
recycling plant of construction boards is very new, and the production and resource use is expected to
improve with time, though this was not taken into account in the model. Data were also obtained from
Fiskeby for their emissions and energy- and resource use in 2022. The waste sampling took place in
2022-2023. Nevertheless, the materials used as input in the model are modelled based on an analysis
from 2009 (Riber et al., 2009), and some aspects of the materials may have changed since then.
==PAGE:68==
6.3 Scope
48
The data should be representative of the next five years. However, in 2025 the EPR for packaging will
be implemented to incentivize producers to make more sustainable packaging, which may affect the
production, design, and resource use of beverage cartons.
The scenarios are modelled with a ”market” process for the energy mix in the respective country.
These are made with assumptions about how the energy will be generated in the future since it is a
consequential LCA. However, these mixes do not represent the current state of energy production in
Denmark, Sweden, or Germany.
Geographical representativeness- UBCs are disposed of in Denmark, and the sorting is done in Denmark
or Germany; this situation is expected to remain the same. While some sorting plants are in the planning
phase, they will be in Denmark, which does not affect this aspect of representativeness. Recycling is
done either in Germany or Sweden. The data obtained are from Sweden’s two recycling plants, which
can be assumed to be representative of the area.
6.3.5
Life cycle interpretation
The results of the assessment, the LCI analysis, and LCIA, are presented and described in Chapter
7. The LCIA results are shown in a characterized and normalized form, using the global normalizing
factors shown in Table 6.2.
The interpretation of the results is done using the outlines provided by Hauschild et al. (2018), and
Laurent et al. (2020).
Completeness and consistency checks are performed to verify the data collected and to assure that
the LCIs are adequately representative for decision-making in accordance with the goal and scope.
Significant issues are identified using contribution analysis and completeness and consistency checks.
Sensitivity analysis is performed in the form of perturbation analysis and scenario analysis. Based
on the perturbation analysis, parameters considered sensitive (−1 ≥SR ≥1) are assessed using data
quality assessment. The assessment is conducted using the pedigree matrix from Weidema et al. (2013)
to assign a rating (DQR) for the sensitive parameters.
Values considered sensitive, which are also
assigned a low DQR, will be tested further using scenario analysis. The energy systems and their effect
on the assessed scenarios will also be tested using scenario analysis.
6.3.5.1
Basis for uncertainty and sensitivity analyses
The LCA models will be tested for their robustness in several ways. First, contribution analysis is
conducted to understand how the impact categories are modelled, which processes affected them, and
in which way. Process contribution analysis was also used to identify the significant issues in the model
to be tested further in the scenario analysis.
A perturbation analysis assessed the model’s sensitivity to the changes in each parameter. This was
done by adding 10% to each parameter, a one-at-a-time change, as described in Clavreul et al. (2012).
The analysis was based on the sensitivity ratio (SR) using the following equation:
SR =
∆result
Initial result
∆parameter
Initial parameter
(6.1)
Parameters are considered sensitive if the −1 ≥SR ≥1, denoting that the 10% perturbation resulted
in a change ≥|10%|.
Based on the perturbation analysis, data quality assessment will be performed on the parameters deemed
sensitive. The assessment will be conducted based on the pedigree matrix presented by Weidema et al.
(2013), where the parameters are evaluated based on their reliability, completeness, and temporal and
geographical correlation, as well as further technological correlation, and awarded a score of 1 to 5 in
==PAGE:69==
6.3 Scope
49
each aspect. The results will then be converted to data quality rating (DQR) based on the equation
used in the ILCD handbook (European Commission, 2010, p. 332), which is modified to the aspects
used and shown below
DQR = Rel + C + Temp + Geo + Tech + Xw · 4
i + 4
(6.2)
where:
Rel = Reliability
C = Completeness
Temp = Temporal correlation
Geo = Geographical correlation
Tech = Further technological correlation
Xw = The aspect with the highest score, i.e., lowest quality
i = number of indicators
The DQR will be converted to an evaluation assigned to each of the parameters, based on the following
key from European Commission (2010):
DQR ≤1.6 is considered high quality
3 ≥DQR > 1.6 is considered basic quality
4 ≥DQR > 3 is considered data estimate.
Lastly, a scenario analysis will be conducted to evaluate the main issues and assumptions in the models,
both based on assumptions, and the contribution analysis, which was used to identify which processes
are significant for each impact category. The calculation of the relative change was done using the
following equation:
% Relative change = Impact scorescenario −Impact scorebase
Impact scorebase
(6.3)

CHAPTER 7
LCA results
This chapter will present the results of the life cycle assessment. First, in form of an LCI analysis. The
results of the LCIA will also be presented, both in form of characterized and normalized results.
7.1
Life Cycle Inventory Analysis
In this section, the modelling of the different systems will be presented, including the assumptions used.
The modelling choices will be compared to other studies published in the literature in accordance with
the goal and scope definitions.
7.1.1
Data requirements and assumptions
The following section will present the data used to carry out this LCA study and assumptions made for
missing data. The section will be divided into the different processes used.
Much of the data was collected through interviews, stakeholder communication, and waste sampling.
The rest was based on data available in the EASETECH library but mainly on the Ecoinvent database,
version 3.7.1, using the consequential version of the database.
Detailed processes for each life cycle stage and Ecoinvent processes used can be seen in the LCI inventory
in Appendix B
7.1.1.1
Post-consumer beverage cartons (UBC)
The input to the models is UBCs, containing residues. The composition of the beverage cartons is
found in the literature. Refrigerated BCs are made of 80% cardboard and 20% plastic (Miljøstyrelsen,
2018). Aseptic cartons are made of 75% cardboard, 20% plastic, and 5% aluminum (Bisinella et al.,
2018; Stramarkou et al., 2021; Pasqualino et al., 2011). These cartons are modelled by the different
material fractions, using physico-chemical material compositions from Riber et al. (2009). The ratio
between refrigerated and aseptic cartons found from the waste sampling conducted in SS waste from
Copenhagen municipality was 72% refrigerated cartons and 28% aseptic cartons. The ratios between
refrigerated and aseptic cartons and the use-based distribution of BC types are outlined in Table 5.6.
The results obtained from the waste sampling are in accordance with the values estimated by the Danish
EPA, which used 76% refrigerated and 24% aseptic to model the Danish system (Miljøstyrelsen, 2020b).
Residues in the UBC
According to Wohner et al. (2019), different uses for BC yield different levels of residues. The waste
sampling confirmed this, and the level of residues in the model was calculated for each product type
with a high occurrence in the waste sampling and for each category, such as milk, yogurt, and cream.
The results of the waste sampling’s level of residues are shown in Section 5.3. The level of residues from
SS UBC was used to determine the general level of residues in the reference flow in order to represent
more accurately the behavior of UBC source segregation. The estimated level of residues in the model,
15.7%, is slightly lower than other European estimates. According to Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017),
==PAGE:71==
7.1 Life Cycle Inventory Analysis
51
the estimated level of residues was 25% in the Netherlands. Zero Waste Europe (2020) estimated dirt
and moisture to be 17%.
The combined composition of cardboard, plastic, aluminum, and organic residues was normalized, i.e.
slightly reduced, using Microsoft Excel to reach 100%. The normalized composition is shown in Table
7.2.
7.1.1.2
Collection and transport
The model for UBC is for post-consumer beverage cartons and begins when the consumers dispose of
the cartons in the waste container. The collection distance can vary between cities. The same is true for
transportation to the incineration plant and transfer stations. Exact distance values for the collection
were not obtained since the models aim to reflect the future situation in Denmark rather than a specific
city. Therefore, values from Miljøstyrelsen (2020b); Bisinella et al. (2018) were used.
Transportation distances to the treatment plants located abroad were calculated with a starting point
in Odense to ensure a fair representation of the different regions in Denmark. Transportation from the
sorting plants in Germany to the recycling plant in Germany was calculated as the distance between
the German sorting plant Hündgen Entsorgungs GmbH & Co. and the recycling plant Papierfabrik
Niederauer Mühle using Google maps. These specific plants were chosen based on data provided by
Combineering (2023). The transport in all scenarios is via trucks. The datasets for emissions related to
transportation and collection were retrieved using the Ecoinvent database. The transportation distances
for each scenario can be seen in Table 7.2, and detailed processes are provided in Appendix B.
7.1.1.3
Transfer stations
A substantial part of the collected UBC in Denmark is sorted and recycled abroad. To minimize the
volume and ensure eﬀicient transportation, the UBC is transported to local transfer stations, where the
waste is baled and packed to be shipped to the sorting plants. Esben Møhl from ARC has provided an
estimate of the electricity consumption of baling and packing. Data regarding packaging materials of
bales were provided by AFLD1, Nordværk, and ARC. In ARC and Nordværk, the waste is packed with
steel wire and plastic wrap, while in AFLD, they use only steel wire. Based on the data, 2 kg of steel
and 2 kg of LDPE were used to bale and pack the reference flow of 1 ton of UBCs.
7.1.1.4
Loss of residues
Loss of residues occurs after the collection of the UBC due to the collection truck’s compactor or other
reasons. This assumption was confirmed by the operation managers in AFLD and ARC.
In an evaluation made in the Netherlands to assess their UBC recycling, they estimated the loss of
residues due to baling and transportation. For PD, the Dutch equivalent to the most common Danish
waste stream of Plastic and UBC, the losses were estimated to be 8% in the sampled municipalities
and 9% for the entire PD category (Thoden van Velzen et al., 2017, pages 99-101). The model in this
report will use the more conservative loss rate of 8% and assume that the loss rate in transportation,
which is also relevant for the incineration scenario, is 3%. The loss rate as a result of the baling process
is 5%. It is assumed that the loss of residues will be incinerated and will, therefore, not influence the
incineration scenario.
7.1.1.5
Incineration
The baseline scenario depicts the incineration of UBCs in Denmark. The data used are from ARC,
which is considered a state-of-the-art incineration plant. Data regarding emissions and residues were
1AFLD is the waste company for the following municipalities: Billund, Hedensted, Herning, Ikast-Brande, Ringkøbing-
Skjern, and Varde
==PAGE:72==
7.1 Life Cycle Inventory Analysis
52
retrieved from Bisinella et al. (2021), and the baseline scenario in the article for ARC is used in this
model. The data provided are for municipal solid waste and its composition, where UBC is one of the
fractions incinerated. The inventory is taken from Bisinella et al. (2021). However, the metals (Mn,
Ni, Cd, Hg, Pb, As) were removed from the process-specific emissions and only used as input-specific
emissions. The energy recovery eﬀiciency is modelled as 25.4% for electricity and 72% for heat; the rest
is assumed to be lost.
7.1.1.6
Sorting
Sorting of UBCs in Denmark occurs in several Danish and German sorting plants.
Some specific
municipalities have their own sorting plant, where the sorting is done primarily manually (Fredericia
and Randers). Other sorting plants in Denmark are not modelled due to a lack of data. The Danish
sorting plant is modelled after the Nordværk sorting plant in Aalborg and is based on data provided
by them. The UBC shipped to Nordværk is collected with metal and plastic. The waste goes through
manual and mechanical sorting, including Eddy current separator, foil separator, and NIR scanner. The
sorting eﬀiciency is reported to be 90% (Nordværk, 2023). The sorting in Germany is modelled after
the German sorting plant, Hündgen Entsorgungs GmbH & Co. German sorting eﬀiciency is reportedly
between 90-95% according to Extr:act (2023), and modelled as 90% eﬀiciency. This fits well with the
reported sorting eﬀiciencies of 90% in Sweden (Markwardt et al., 2017) and 89% eﬀiciency in Germany
(Zero Waste Europe, 2020).
The electricity use for each of the sorting plants modelled in Denmark and Germany was provided by
the sorting plants. The energy use in the sorting plants is modelled after data obtained from Nordværk
and Combineering for Hündgen Entsorgungs to be 65 and 75 kWh per ton, respectively. The energy
consumption is higher than that found in the literature. According to Abejón et al. (2020), the electricity
consumption is about 51 kWh, but the difference is minimal.
7.1.1.7
Recycling
In this study, different recycling scenarios for UBCs are assessed. Data regarding the recycling processes
and eﬀiciencies were obtained through literature and personal communication with the recycling plants.
Data for the recovered materials were obtained from the Ecoinvent database. The recycling plants for
cardboard fibers in Sweden and Germany are modelled based on data provided by Fiskeby. In these
plants, the sorted UBCs undergo hydropulping, where the cardboard fibers are separated from the PE-
Al fractions. It is modelled so that only the fibers are recovered. The PE-Al fractions are assumed to
be incinerated for energy recovery. The recycling eﬀiciency obtained from the literature is calculated
as 69-70% (Markwardt et al., 2017; Miljøstyrelsen, 2019). The recycling plants for cardboard fibers are
assumed to recover 70% of the cardboard fibers, and the remainder is treated as residues and incinerated
locally.
The substitution of the primary materials in the recycling processes is based on their functionality. The
substitution ratio or market response indicates the extent of the virgin material substitution. This was
found in the literature to be 0.9 (Schlecht & Wellenreuther, 2020), indicating that 1 kg of recycled
cardboard will substitute 0.9 kg of virgin cardboard. This ratio is used in the model.
The other form of secondary recycling is modelled upon Recoma in Hässleholm, Sweden. Here, sorted
UBCs are shredded together with PE-Al reject from other recycling plants and are made into construc-
tion boards. The materials are flattened and heated to eliminate the organic residues in the UBC and
the water content. The shredded materials are used as filling for the boards. The filling is coated with
an outer layer of recycled paperboard and laminated with LDPE (Recoma, 2022). The lifetime of the
boards is lab tested to exceed 50 years, and according to the company, used boards can be re-fed into
the system and used as filling in new boards production.
To comply with the system boundaries, it is assumed that the recovered mass of UBC substitutes a mix
of virgin pulpwood and LDPE in a ratio of 75% and 25%, respectively. Recoma estimated the board
==PAGE:73==
7.1 Life Cycle Inventory Analysis
53
density at 750-850 kg/m3. Different wood types have different densities (The Engineering Toolbox,
n.d.) within this range, so it is assumed that the UBC mass will replace the same amount of pulpwood
based on a density of 750 kg/m3.
7.1.1.8
Treatment of residues
Residues are left from most processes. The residues from the sorting, baling, and transport processes
are assumed to be collected and sent to incineration, including the organic residue loss in transport and
baling. The treatment of residues from the incineration process is modelled on the treatment detailed
in Bisinella et al. (2018), using EASETECH models and processes from the Ecoinvent database.
Treatment of residues from the recycling processes in form of incineration abroad was modelled using
Ecoinvent processes.
It is assumed that the construction boards’ production harbor no residues from the recycling processes
since organic residues are treated with heat and evaporate or become inert. Production residues of the
construction boards are re-fed into the system and used for new boards.
7.1.1.9
Material and energy requirements
Additional materials and energy supplies were modelled using Ecoinvent processes. If possible, ”market”
processes were used rather than ”production datasets” to illustrate the change that can arise due to the
model. Processes from Denmark, Sweden, and Germany were prioritized for the respective scenarios.
The market databases for the specific geographical location were applied to the extent possible. When
the specific location processes were not possible, European processes were used, and in some cases,
global, when there were no better options. Table 7.1 provides the composition of the energy mixes in
the market processes for Denmark, Sweden, and Germany used in the models. The data are taken from
Wernet et al. (2016).
Table 7.1: Presentation of the energy mixes as used in the market processes for Denmark, Sweden,
and Germany. The data is taken from Ecoinvent 3.7.1

7.2 Life Cycle Impact Assessment
55
7.2
Life Cycle Impact Assessment
The life cycle impact assessment (LCIA) is made using the Environmental Footprint 3.0 with long-term
impact with toxicity. The assessment was based on both characterized and normalized results for the 16
assessed impact categories. The characterized results show the results in relevant units for each impact
category, while the normalized results are based on the European Person Equivalent (PE). The PE
describes the impact associated with the average European resident from various activities, which could
be helpful to set the models’ results in context (Sala et al., 2017). Table 7.3 shows the characterized
results for each of the four assessed scenarios. The scenarios are named to make it easier to distinguish
between them, as shown in Section 6.2.
Table 7.3: The characterized results of the assessed scenarios in the LCA. INC stands for incineration
of the waste fraction. Marked with green are the lower results for each impact category, while red marks
the higher results for each impact category. The colors are used as a scale, where the darker shades
indicate the highest/lowest for each category. Negative values indicate savings to the environment, while
positive indicate burdens as a result of the scenario.

As seen in Table 7.3, there is not a clear answer as to which of the waste treatment models is the
most environmentally sustainable since neither of them performs best in all 16 impact categories. For
CC, Recoma shows the largest saving of -280 kg CO2 eq. for the reference flow. The incineration
scenario indicates a burden to the environment concerning CC. That is partly due to the energy system
used. A more in-depth assessment of the impact of the energy system, can be found in the scenario
analysis in Section 8.5.4.2. Recoma also shows the largest environmental saving concerning the use of
fossil resources (RDfos) and ionizing radiation (IR). The incineration scenario, while scoring the lowest
score concerning CC, shows the highest savings to the environment, as seen in 8 of the 16 assessed
impact categories. The results of the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios are similar due to the modelling
choices. They differ in the local energy sources used and the distances to the sorting and recycling
plants but are both modelled after Fiskeby board in Sweden. The Fiskeby scenario shows the highest
savings for particulate matter (PM), Acidification (TA), and Land use (LU). The water use (WU)
savings are also higher in Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios than in INC and Recoma scenarios. The
Fiskeby scenario generally shows slightly better results than Hündgen. This can be explained by the
transportation distances, which are longer in the Hündgen scenario compared to Fiskeby. Moreover, the
German sorting plant is modelled to use more energy per ton of waste. The reason for the difference
==PAGE:76==
7.2 Life Cycle Impact Assessment
56
can, however, also originate from the energy system in Germany.
According to Fazio et al. (2018); Sala et al. (2017), the impact categories have different levels of recom-
mendation to indicate whether the data are satisfactory and recommended or that recommendations
based on the specific category should be applied with caution. The three impact categories considered
satisfactory are CC, OD, and PM. If the assessment should be based exclusively on these three cat-
egories, then the Recoma scenario will be considered the most sustainable. Recoma seems to be the
most sustainable scenario since it creates environmental savings in 15 out of 16 impact categories. The
burden Recoma presents for OD is estimated at 1.1 · 10−4 PE per ton of UBC waste.
Figure 7.1 illustrates the results in a normalized form to make them comparable and set the results in
context. The values used in this figure can be seen in Appendix C.
Figure 7.1: The results of the four assessed scenarios in a normalized form, given in PE
As seen in Figure 7.1, there are two impact categories that noticeably stand out. FE shows that the
Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios, that is, recycling of cardboard fibers, create a considerable environ-
mental saving in Freshwater Eutrophication. ET illustrates saving to the environment, both from the
incineration and Recoma scenarios, but the incineration yields much higher savings.
On the other
hand, the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios create a relatively heavy environmental burden regarding
ET, mainly due to the emissions to water from the recycling processes. Distinct results can also be seen
for Recoma showing savings regarding resource use (RDfos and RD). Incineration also yields notable
savings regarding RD. The environmental impact of the scenarios in most impact categories is diﬀicult
to discern in the figure and represents relatively small savings or burdens. It is to be expected since the
waste management of 1 ton UBC including residues and will not compare to the annual activities of a
European resident. Figure 7.2 shows the same results on a more narrow y-axis to be able to discern the
results from the impact categories.
All in all, the different waste treatment scenarios yield mostly savings to the environment in the different
impact categories. However, the waste treatment scenarios create burdens to the environment in five of
the impact categories. Most of the burdens originate from the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios. These
results are highlighted in Table C.2.
==PAGE:77==
