is modified from Figure 7.1 to discern the different results with smaller magnitudes. As a result of that,
some of the bars are cut from this figure
Figure 7.2 illustrates that while the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios create a burden to the environment
in some impact categories, they also yield larger savings than Recoma and the incineration scenarios
concerning PM, LU, and WU.
==PAGE:78==
CHAPTER 8
LCA interpretation
This chapter will analyze and interpret the LCA results shown in Chapter 7. Completeness and consis-
tency checks are performed to ensure the data quality of the assessment and to evaluate whether the
assessment is consistent with the goal and scope. These checks are done qualitatively. The LCIA will
be analyzed using a contribution analysis to identify the significant processes for each scenario. The
contribution analysis is part of the iterative nature of the LCA, as illustrated in Figure 6.1, that enables
further improvement of the assessment. Furthermore, Lastly, a quantitative sensitivity analysis is per-
formed, including perturbation- and scenario analysis, and data quality assessment. The limitations of
the LCA will be discussed as part of the discussion in Chapter 9. Conclusions and recommendations are
also part of the interpretation phase, as outlined by Hauschild et al. (2018), but these will be presented
in Chapter 10 and include the conclusions for the entire study and not just the LCA segment.
8.1
Completeness check
The completeness check is performed for the LCI and LCIA to verify that the data used and the
modelling choices performed are adequate and suﬀicient to reach a conclusion in accordance with the
goal and scope of the study. Nevertheless, there will always be some unknown variables, making it
diﬀicult to assess the completeness as a whole.
Efforts were made to find appropriate and specific data to represent the Danish waste treatment system
of UBC accurately. The material generation used as reference flow in this LCA is based on the waste
sampling conducted in Copenhagen. It is assumed to be reliable and suﬀiciently representative for a
study of this scale.
The LCA includes the three most common scenarios of UBC waste treatment: incineration, sorting
in Denmark and recycling in Sweden, and sorting and recycling in Germany.
Not all sorting and
recycling plants are included and modelled due to time and data constraints, which will affect the
representativeness of the models since the goal is to evaluate waste treatment for Denmark.
Since no impact categories were excluded, it can be assumed that the LCIA encompasses all the essential
information for the study.
For the Recoma scenario, neither emissions were modelled other than from the energy use nor residues.
Heating the mass of shredded UBC will cause emissions, which are yet to be measured.
The vast
majority of the production residues are re-fed into the system. While it is probable that some residues
are disposed of differently, this is assumed to be of a minimal magnitude that will not affect the overall
results.
The results are deemed uncertain to some degree due to data gaps identified, mainly for recycling.
Identification of eventual data gaps was also one of the objectives of the whole study. Overall, considering
the system approach of this study, the completeness is suﬀicient to reach preliminary conclusions within
the limitations and uncertainties discussed.
==PAGE:79==
8.2 Consistency check
59
8.2
Consistency check
The consistency check is conducted to evaluate whether the data, methods, and assumptions used in
the assessment are consistent with the goal and scope.
The same LCI modelling framework and LCIA methodology were applied to all the assessed scenar-
ios. Data was collected from different sources through literature, involved stakeholder, sampling, and
assumptions/estimates. First-hand data was preferred since it is assumed to be the best quality. The
level of precision is different between the four assessed scenarios. Detailed data concerning input and
emissions were obtained for Fiskeby and ARC. The data in the Hündgen scenario is obtained from a
German sorting plant and from Fiskeby board in Sweden. The data about recycling processes from
Fiskeby board was used to model the German recycling processes, even though they use different tech-
nology1. German boundary conditions were applied to adjust the recycling processes in Germany. For
the Recoma scenario, only the electricity use was modelled for the recycling process due to a lack of
additional data. These may give a partial picture of the processes.
Geographical and socio-economic conditions may cause variations in the distribution of BC types and
levels of residues. An increase in the level of residues was evaluated in the scenario analysis. The
incineration data is also from Copenhagen (ARC), but it is considered a state-of-the-art incineration
plant.
A mistake was made in the substitution of cardboard from the recovered fibers, substituting a recycled
material instead of primary material, as intended.
The substitution effect was investigated in the
scenario analysis and evaluated to have a small impact on the overall results. Moreover, the energy
consumption in the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios is based primarily on fuel energy. The models used
diesel as fuel since the fuel used in Fiskeby, a biofuel that is supposed to be much more sustainable,
could not be modelled using Ecoinvent processes. The recycling scenarios did not address the steel wire
used for the baling. This is especially problematic in the Recoma scenario, where residue treatment is
not modelled.
The sorting eﬀiciency was set for all recycling models at 90%. The high eﬀiciency is only valid for some
of the Danish sorting plants but is assumed to be reliable for the modelled plant. However, it should
be remembered that the lifetime of UBC is relatively short- somewhere between 6 weeks (Econet, 2021)
and months (Extr:act, 2023). During the summer, the lifetime is even shorter since the heat brings out
maggots and causes faster deterioration of the material Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017). While this
concern can affect the sorting and recycling rates, it was not considered in the scenarios, as sorting and
recycling plants are working on solving the issue.
The collected data were compared to similar studies, as seen in Section 7.1.1. Values such as the level of
residues in UBC, energy consumption in the sorting plant, recycling rate, and sorting eﬀiciency are all
compared to existing literature. A previous study made on beverage packaging in Denmark (Bisinella
et al., 2018) was used to model processes where data gaps were found and compared to the first-hand
collected data. All the data used in the models appear to have the same geographical and temporal
scope and seem to be applied consistently.
The assessment was done in a consistent way, and the results are assumed to be suﬀicient to reach
preliminary conclusions within the limitations and uncertainties discussed.
1In Fiskeby board, UBCs are recycled with paper and cardboard.
In Germany, UBCs are recycled in dedicated
recycling plants. Therefore, it is assumed that the recycling processes are different
==PAGE:80==
8.3 Contribution analysis
60
8.3
Contribution analysis
In this section, significant issues that could potentially change the final results of the study will be
identified using contribution analysis.
In this type of analysis, the contribution from each stage is
quantified and expressed graphically for each of the assessed scenarios. Since an LCA is an iterative
study, this analysis was done several times during the execution of the study, and the models and results
were assessed and, in some cases, modified. The different processes are shown using their relative share
of the total impact, also called dominance analysis (Hauschild et al., 2018).
Figure 8.1 shows the process contribution analysis to the incineration scenario. The impact categories
are shown in Table 6.2.
Figure 8.1:
The process contribution analysis for the incineration of one ton of UBC, including
residues. The results are given for the 16 assessed impact categories.
As Figure 8.1 clearly illustrates, most of the savings in the scenario come from heat substitution. The
displaced heat is modelled as heat pumps running on electricity. The substitution of electricity also
contributes to environmental savings in the different impact categories. The burden to the environment
concerning CC, originates from the incineration process. where the largest contributor (40%) is the use
of calcium carbonate at the plant. The modelled energy mix is as shown in Table 7.1 approximately
60% wind energy and 40% use of wood, which is less carbon intensive than incineration and can explain
why incineration of the waste yields a burden rather than a saving as often expected.
The management of incineration residues has minimal impact on the different categories. However, the
municipal collection contributes to the environmental burden of the scenario, which can be expected,
especially considering IR, POF, OD, and PM. The model used 20 km as the distance for collection
and transport to the incineration plant, based on an assumption from Miljøstyrelsen (2020b), with
diesel collection trucks. The incineration scenario generally shows more savings than burdens to the
environment for all but the CC impact category, as shown in the normalized results in Table C.2.
==PAGE:81==
8.3 Contribution analysis
61
It is also interesting to see that most of the impact in this scenario comes from the background system,
energy, and materials recovery, while the foreground system, including the collection and incineration,
has a smaller impact on the scenario.
The contribution analysis is shown for the foreground and
background systems in Appendix D, Figure D.1.
Figure 8.2: The process contribution analysis for the Fiskeby scenario for recovery of cardboard fibers
in Sweden from one ton of UBC, including residues. The results are given for the 16 assessed impact
categories.
Figure 8.2 illustrates the process contribution analysis for the Fiskeby scenario. The waste is sorted
in Denmark and shipped to Sweden, where the cardboard fibers are recycled, and the remainder is
incinerated in Sweden.
It is clear to see that the cardboard substitution from the recovery of fibers contributes greatly to
the environmental savings in most of the impact categories. Nevertheless, it poses a burden to the
environment considering IR and ET, because the substituted product is modelled to yield savings in
these impact categories2. Therefore, substituting the product will create a burden in the system.
The recycling process yields most of the burdens in this scenario, especially for CC, OD, IR, and
ET, because cardboard production requires the use of chemicals that end up contaminating freshwater
(Markwardt et al., 2017). The incineration processes in Denmark and Sweden add to the savings as
a result of the scenario by substituting energy and materials. The incineration in Denmark distinctly
contributes as savings for RD due to electricity and heat substitution.
The sorting processes have
minimal impact on the overall results, except for HTc, where the negative contribution originates from
the use of steel to bale the waste (77% of the impact of the sorting processes). The collection and
transport contribute further to the burdens of the scenario, ostensibly since the distance used in the
model to the recycling plant is 655 km.
2These savings, which become burdens in this study, are part of the external process used and are not modelled
specifically for this assessment
==PAGE:82==
8.3 Contribution analysis
62
Unlike the incineration scenario, in the Fiskeby scenario, it is the background processes for materials that
are substantial for the impacts of the scenario. The contribution analysis is shown for the foreground
and background systems in Appendix D, Figure D.3.
Figure 8.3: The process contribution analysis for the Hündgen scenario for recovery of cardboard fibers
in Germany from one ton of UBC, including residues. The results are given for the 16 assessed impact
categories.
Figure 8.3 presents the process contribution analysis for the Hündgen scenario. The waste is collected
in Denmark and sent to local transfer stations, where it is baled and packed for shipping to a sorting
plant in Germany. From there, the waste is baled again and shipped to a nearby recycling plant, where
the cardboard fibers are recycled, and the remainder is incinerated in Germany.
Similar to the Fiskeby scenario, the cardboard substitution from the recovery of fibers contributes
greatly to the savings in most of the impact categories but also causes a burden to the environment
considering IR and ET. The recycling process yields most of the burdens in this scenario, especially for
HTc and ET. While the scenario generally yields an environmental saving, it also yields a burden for
four of the impact categories: OD, HTc, IR, and ET. The same is true for the Fiskeby scenario.
The incineration processes in Denmark have minimal impact on the results. So does the sorting in
Germany, but that may be due to the modelling choices since only electricity use and the use of steel
wire are modelled for the sorting processes. Nevertheless, the impact of the sorting process on HTc is
notable. It originates primarily from the use of steel to bale the waste (79% of the emissions for the
sorting processes). On the other hand, the collection and transfer stations in Denmark do contribute to
the burdens of the scenario, mainly due to the municipal collection. The transportation to the sorting
and recycling plants is modelled as 776 km to the sorting plant and another 40 km to the recycling plant,
which can be seen as part of the burdens in this scenario. The incineration in Germany generally adds
==PAGE:83==
8.3 Contribution analysis
63
to the savings as a result of the scenario, where 82% of the contribution originates from the incineration
of the recycling residues, i.e. the remainder of the cardboard fibers and PE-Al fractions.
Similarly to the Fiskeby scenario, the background processes for materials have a substantial impact on
the overall results of the Hündgen scenario. Nevertheless, the background processes for energy are more
pronounced in Germany than in Sweden. The contribution analysis is shown for the foreground and
background systems in Appendix D, Figure D.4
Figure 8.4: The process contribution analysis for the Recoma scenario for recycling whole UBCs to
construction board materials. The analysis is shown for one ton of UBC, including residues. The results
are given for the 16 assessed impact categories.
Figure 8.4 presents the process contribution analysis for the Recoma scenario. The waste is collected
and sorted in Denmark, then baled, packed, and shipped to Sweden, where the waste is recycled
into construction boards. To comply with the system boundaries, it is modelled to substitute virgin
pulpwood and plastic to be used as filling for construction boards.
It is abundantly clear from the figure that the substitution of virgin materials yields most of the savings
in the scenario. In 15 impact categories, the environmental savings are due to the substitution of virgin
PE (between 79% in OD to 99% in WU, RD, and RDfos). Still, the substitution of virgin pulpwood
contributes 97% of the savings in LU.
The recycling process poses a burden to the environment, but the overall contribution of the process
is small. The incineration of losses during transport and baling, along with the sorting residues, add
savings to the scenario. Most of the burdens in the Recoma scenario originate from the collection and
transport processes. The sorting processes do contribute to the overall impacts, mostly to HTc, again
due to the steel wire, which also causes a burden in FE. The ET burdens are equally divided between
electricity use and steel wire.
Generally, the Recoma scenario presents savings to the environment in all but one impact category,
OD, where it is a burden, mostly due to the collection of waste and transport to the recycling plant in
==PAGE:84==
8.4 Perturbation analysis and data quality assessment
64
Sweden, a distance of 294 km. Though the impact of Recoma to OD is 1.1 · 10−4, so it is a burden but
on a very small scale.
The background processes for energy contribute to the savings in this scenario, but the material sub-
stitution dominates the overall impacts. The contribution analysis is shown for the foreground and
background systems in Appendix D, Figure D.2
8.3.1
Summary of the significant issues
Based on the contribution analysis shown for each of the assessed scenarios, several significant issues
were identified for further examination under sensitivity and scenario analysis:
• Energy system used- with the used energy mix, incineration represents a burden to the environ-
ment considering CC, which invites further examination
• Heat substitution - The heat substitution generally shows substantial environmental savings in
the incineration scenario and contributes to the environmental savings in the remaining scenario
as well.
• Cardboard substitution- shows substantial savings in the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios but is
also a burden in some impact categories.
• Construction board material substitution- has the largest impact on the Recoma scenario.
8.4
Perturbation analysis and data quality assessment
Perturbation analysis is used in this study to find the sensitive parameters and identify potential issues
in the model. The perturbation analysis is done as a one-at-a-time change of 10% to estimate an SR.
A parameter is considered sensitive if −1 ≥SR ≥1.
The perturbation analysis was conducted on 114 unique parameters used in the four different scenarios
using EASETECH. The perturbation was analyzed for ±10% of the initial result and is done for all
impact categories.
Table 8.1 shows the number of parameters assessed in each scenario and for the LCA in total.
Table 8.1: Overview of the perturbation analysis made in this study. Repeated parameters indicate
parameters that are used in more than one scenario.

The perturbation analysis shown in Table 8.2 shows that the incineration scenario is the least sensitive
scenario, which is quite surprising, due to its simplicity, compared to the other scenarios. The percentage
of heat recovery is the sensitive parameter in the incineration scenario, due to the large impact the heat
substitution has on the overall impact score in the incineration scenario, as shown in figure 8.1. Heat
recovery yields SR scores of −1 ≥SR ≥1 for OD, IR, WU, and RDfos, and high SR scores in the
remaining impact categories as well.
Heat recovery is evaluated as sensitive in the Fiskeby and Recoma scenarios as well, though not for the
same impact category (HTc for Fiskeby, and OD for Recoma).
Fiskeby is the most sensitive scenario, with 16 parameters deemed sensitive, though it should be noted
that 10 of these parameters are only sensitive due to HTc. HTc for Fiskeby scenario is estimated to be
7.2 · 10−9 CTUh. So while changes may occur in this impact category, they also may be insignificant
(see illustration of the HTc in a normalized form in Figure 7.2). The parameter most sensitive in the
scenario, i.e., sensitive in the largest number of impact categories, is the substitution factor of cardboard,
which has a substantial impact on the scenario, as shown in Figure 8.2. Also sensitive is the percentage
of recycling eﬀiciency, i.e., how much of the cardboard fibers are recovered in the recycling process. This
value was set at 70% as a conservative estimate after discussion with involved stakeholders. Lastly, the
two parameters considered sensitive are the loss of materials under baling, which was evaluated after
==PAGE:86==
8.4 Perturbation analysis and data quality assessment
66
observations in transfer stations, and sorting eﬀiciency.
The Hündgen scenario contains seven sensitive parameters. Substitution of cardboard, recovery of fibers,
sorting eﬀiciency, and loss of materials under baling are also sensitive in this scenario. Six out of the
seven parameters are sensitive considering CC.
The Recoma scenario consists of eight sensitive parameters out of the 68 assessed. OD is sensitive
in all these parameters but is also estimated to be 2.6 · 10−6, so changes, while sensitive, may be
insignificant.
It should be noted that OD is the only impact category where the Recoma scenario
presents an environmental burden. Similar to the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios, the Recoma scenario
is sensitive to sorting eﬀiciency and loss of materials under baling.
None of the scenarios is sensitive to any of the parameters used to model the management of incineration
residues: fly ash, bottom ash, and aluminum.
8.4.1
Data quality assessment
Based on the perturbation analysis, a data quality assessment was performed. A more eﬀicient approach
was used, where only the parameters considered sensitive were evaluated using the pedigree matrix from
Weidema et al. (2013), and assigned a DQR.
The list of sensitive parameters, their descriptions and their corresponding DQR is shown in Table 8.3.
Of the 22 assessed unique parameters3, one was concluded to be high quality, the electricity consumption
at Recoma. This value was given by Recoma and is based on their measurements.
12 parameters are assumed to be basic quality. Some parameters such as distances to treatment plants,
recovery eﬀiciency, or amount of steel wire are not estimates, but rather concrete data. Nevertheless,
the completeness aspect, seeing as the data originate from one treatment plant, causes the DQR to be
reduced to basic quality.
Nine parameters are considered data estimates because they are actual estimates and calculations to
cover data gaps and test assumptions. The baling process is modelled on no losses other than organic
residues. The parameter was made to test the effect of such loss due to observations in the transfer
stations. Incineration of residues in Sweden was also made to test the effect of the process. Substitution
of virgin materials in construction board filling was made on estimates to comply with the system
boundaries of primary material substitution.
A complete list of the assessed parameters, the assigned scores for each of the aspects, as well as the
argument to assign the specific scores can be seen in Appendix I
3many of the parameters assessed sensitive were repeated parameters that were used in more than one scenario

The sensitive parameters were also evaluated in regard to the contribution analysis. The collection
parameter, even though considered sensitive, have a small impact on the scenarios, and will, therefore,
not be evaluated further. Loss of residues during transport and baling is bound to happen, so it would
not be examined further. The same is true for the loss of materials under the baling process. It was not
possible to acquire more data at that point, but in some cases of sensitive parameters considered data
estimates, which have an impact on the specific scenario, and a change is realistic, a scenario analysis
was made to evaluate the issue. Such cases are the fiber recovery and substitution of cardboard for the
Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios.
==PAGE:88==
8.5 Scenario analysis
68
8.5
Scenario analysis
The scenario analysis is used in this study to test some main assumptions in the models and to verify
that the results are suﬀiciently robust to lead to meaningful conclusions. It is also used to test whether
the ranking of the scenarios has changed by the result. The scenarios chosen to test in this section are
chosen out of the significant issues found in the contribution- and perturbation analysis.
8.5.1
Increased level of residues
The level of residues in the UBCs is estimated to be between 16-21% according to Table 5.10. In the
model, the lowest value of residues was used, but in this scenario, the environmental impact of UBC
treatment was evaluated for the highest level of residues estimated. The tables with the results can be
seen in Section F.1.
For the incineration, Fiskeby, and Hündgen scenarios, the overall results are worse for the environment
than in the original model. This makes sense since it means fewer cardboard fibers to be recovered
in the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios. It also means that the waste has a lower heating value, which
makes incineration less eﬀicient.
At first glance, it seems peculiar that the results of the Recoma scenarios are slightly improved, but it
is a false understanding. It can be explained by the fact that more UBC waste is used per board when
it contains more residues. Therefore, the energy consumption, which is calculated per board, will be
reduced per ton of UBC waste. On the other hand, it will also mean that fewer boards are produced,
which will affect the financial aspect of the recycling processes.
Overall, and not surprisingly, more residues in the UBCs are not wanted, considering the waste treat-
ment. The effect of the residues in these scenarios is not astounding as the results presented by Wohner
et al. (2019), who presented increases in the range of 50-1800% in the environmental impact of packaging
materials due to residues. Nevertheless, more residues make recycling and incineration less eﬀicient.
8.5.2
Improved fiber recovery
Fiber recovery was set to 70% for the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios, based on discussions with involved
stakeholders. Perturbation analysis showed that it was a sensitive parameter for both the Fiskeby and
Hündgen scenarios, and it was assigned a DQR of ”data estimate”. Therefore, the consequences of
improved fiber recovery were tested by increasing the recovery to 85%. The characterized results of the
Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios are shown with both recovery rates and the relative change in Section
F.2, Table F.3.
As seen in Table F.3, the improved fiber recovery entailed improved environmental impact in 14 out
of the 16 assessed impact categories. The improved recovery in the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios
improved CC by 88-116%, respectively, but also yielded an increased substantial burden concerning
ET of 59-40%, respectively. This may, however, be an artificial increase, since the substituted product
poses a savings to the environment, as discussed earlier under Figure 8.2.
The results were also compared with the original scenarios for Recoma and incineration to test whether
the rankings of the different scenarios have changed as a result of the improved fiber recovery. The
results are shown in Appendix F.2, Table F.4. While the results of the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios
have improved, when compared to the incineration and Recoma scenarios, the ranking of the scenarios
has not changed, except for POF. For POF, the Fiskeby and Hündgen yield a higher saving with the
improved fiber recovery than the incineration, which had the best results in the original model.
==PAGE:89==
8.5 Scenario analysis
69
8.5.3
Substitution of cardboard
The substitution of cardboard has a substantial impact on the overall results of the Fiskeby and Hündgen
scenarios, as shown in the contribution analysis (Figures 8.2 and 8.3).
As described in Section 8.2, a mistake was made, and the recovered fibers were modelled to substitute
a recycled type of cardboard, but with a substitution ratio of a virgin material (0.9). Therefore, several
different Ecoinvent processes were tested to see whether substitution of a different type of cardboard
will make a difference in the overall results. Seven scenarios were tested, where for recycled cardboard,
substitution ratios of 0.9 and 1 were tested.
In the model, it was assumed that a recycled fluting
medium is substituted with a ratio of 0.9. The list of the different cardboard types assessed and their
corresponding Ecoinvent processes can be seen in Appendix F.3.
The results of the different substitution products are illustrated in Figure 8.5. A more narrow scale was
used to be able to discern the differences in the different impact categories. A full-scale of Figure 8.5 is
shown in Appendix F.3, Figure F.1.
Since the scenario analysis for the Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios is very similar, the figure only
depicts the impacts of the Fiskeby scenario. The equivalent figure for the Hündgen scenario and the
characterized results can be seen in Appendix F.3.
Figure 8.5: The normalized results of the Fiskeby scenario using the 5 different cardboard substitutions
where 2 of them have 2 assessed substitution rates. The figure is cut to show the differences between
the scenarios in the impact categories. The full-scale figure and the characterized results can be seen in
Section F.3
It can be seen that, generally, the different cardboard processes follow the same tendencies with varia-
tions in the magnitude of impact. The primary materials, kraftliner, and semi-chemical fluting medium
are the exception concerning CC and ET. While substituting the other processes gives an overall saving
considering CC, the CC impact of the primary materials is smaller than the burdens in the recycling
process, resulting in an overall burden to the environment. On the contrary, the substitution of the
primary materials poses an overall savings considering ET, while it yields a burden for the other types
of cardboard assessed. Nevertheless, the substitution of kraftliner and semi-chemical fluting medium
resulted in worse results in 10 and 11 impact categories, respectively. A substitution rate of 1 for the
==PAGE:90==
8.5 Scenario analysis
70
used process of fluting medium gives overall better results than with a substitution rate of 0.9, except
for ET and IR, where the burden is increased as a result of the scenario.
Assuming the market process with a substitution ratio of 0.9 was the right process to be used in the
original model, the modified Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios are compared to the incineration and
Recoma scenarios in Appendix F.3, Table F.8. The table shows that the rankings of the scenarios do
not change for most of the impact categories. Nevertheless, the modified Fiskeby scenario yields the
highest environmental saving in POF and ME, where it was the incineration in the original model.
8.5.4
Energy mixes
The contribution analysis has shown that the electricity and heat substitution, hence the energy mix,
has a substantial impact on the scenario. As described in Section 6.3.3, the energy mixes used to model
the systems will be tested due to the uncertainty revolving prediction of energy sources in the future.
Three energy systems were tested for Denmark, Sweden, and Germany. The first is the market process
from Ecoinvent, extrapolated from data from 2014, and used in the original model. The second predic-
tion for future energy mix is taken from Muñoz & Weidema (2021) and extrapolated from Denmark’s
energy use between 2014-2019. The third is not a prediction but the actual energy use in 2021, as given
by the IEA country data explorers (IEA, n.d.).
Note: The third scenario is an unusual scenario to assess. It follows the attributional approach of LCA
that uses average processes, while this study uses otherwise the consequential approach. Nevertheless,
the scenario is made to provide involved stakeholders with an answer about the current impact of sorting
and recycling UBC, and not only in the future.
Table 8.4 shows the composition of the three assessed energy mixes. Wind energy is not divided into
onshore and offshore by Muñoz & Weidema (2021), and is modelled as wind, onshore. Wind and solar
are summed up in the IEA summary and are modelled as wind, onshore. Biofuels include waste in the
IEA summaries and are modelled as wood. The equivalent tables for Sweden and Germany and the
Ecoinvent processes used to model the mixes are shown in Section F.4

As seen in Table 8.4, the current energy system consists of more fossil fuels than what is predicted in
the future. For both future scenarios, the energy system is expected to rely much more on wind energy
than it does today. In 2021, 56% of the energy production in Denmark came from fossil fuels.
==PAGE:91==
8.5 Scenario analysis
71
The heat substitution was modified to accommodate the current situation, and instead of being 100%
produced by electricity, it was modelled to be produced from 73% electricity and 27% biomass, based
on the baseline provided by Bisinella et al. (2021). See the details in Section F.4, Table F.10.
The energy mix for Sweden and Germany are shown in Appendix F.4, Tables F.11 and F.12, respectively.
In 2021, fossil fuels were used for 78% of the energy system in Germany, but only 25% of the energy in
Sweden.
8.5.4.1
Future scenarios
The modified results of the four assessed scenarios due to the change in the energy mix as proposed by
Muñoz & Weidema (2021), and the relative changes are shown in Appendix F.4, Tables F.13 and F.14.
Comparing the two predictions for energy mix in the future shows that all four scenarios improve their
CC savings with the energy mix by Muñoz & Weidema (2021). The difference between the future
scenarios for Denmark is that the mix composed by Muñoz & Weidema (2021) uses less wind energy
than the market process (45% compared to 61%). The other major source, according to Muñoz &
Weidema (2021), is natural gas (24%), whereas, at the market process, it is wood (39%)s.
The most substantial difference in the results is for the incineration scenario, which goes from +239 to
-135 kg CO2 eq. per ton UBC. The CC savings are also apparent for the Recoma and Fiskeby scenarios,
with further savings of 18% and 31%, respectively. The OD impact is also considerably improved for
the incineration and Recoma scenarios, with a much more modest reduction in the Fiskeby scenario.
For the incineration scenario, WU and RDfos are also substantially improved with the new mix.
The Hündgen scenario does not change much between the future scenarios, except for RDfos, where the
new energy mix yields a further saving of 60%. This can be explained by the differences between the
two future scenarios for Germany are not as pronounced as for Denmark and Sweden.
The characterized results of the four modified scenarios are shown and compared in Table F.15. As seen
in the Table, the Recoma scenario still yields the highest savings of CC, POF, and RDfos. The Fiskeby
and Hündgen scenarios yield higher savings than the original model. Yet, the higher CC saving from
the incineration scenario, causes the Hündgen scenario to have the worst performance of the assessed
scenarios. As for burdens, the incineration scenario presents only savings to the environment using this
energy mix. The Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios continue to present burdens in four impact categories:
OD, HTc, IR, and ET. The Recoma scenario presents a burden to the environment from one impact
category, but it shifts from OD in the original model to IR.
8.5.4.2
Current energy system
The modified results of the four assessed scenarios due to the change in the energy mix to reflect the
energy use in 2021 based on data obtained from IEA (n.d.), and the relative changes are shown in
Appendix F.4, Tables F.16 and F.17.
Since the current energy system relies heavily on fossil fuels, the CC impact of incineration of the
UBC is substantially affected. Modelling the system with the current energy use, and heat, yields a
substantial saving of CC in the incineration. The CC of incineration is reduced from +239 to -949 kg
CO2 eq. The environmental savings in RDfos are increased by 539% because the incineration of the
UBC waste cardboard replaces fossil fuels.
Generally, the environmental savings in the incineration scenario are increased in 13 out of 16 categories,
where in most of them, a saving of more than 100% is noted. The savings to the environment are reduced
concerning HTc, WU, and RD.
The environmental savings in the Fiskeby and Recoma scenarios are increased in eight and nine impact
categories, respectively. The burden for IR is increased by 53% in Fiskeby. Recoma shifts from pre-
senting an environmental saving in the IR to a burden to the environment. Yet, the previous burden
==PAGE:92==
8.5 Scenario analysis
72
Recoma presented of OD shifts to environmental saving. The environmental savings of CC are also
considerably improved in the Fiskeby and Recoma scenarios.
The most interesting change is in the Hündgen scenario, where the CC saving is reduced by 90%, and the
environmental impact of the recycling scenario is worsened in 15 out of 16 categories. RD is improved
by 79%, but otherwise, the savings are reduced, and the burdens as a result of recycling are increased.
This may be due to the fact that the energy system in Germany in 2021 relied mostly on fossil fuels,
meaning that the benefits of recycling are heavily reduced to the burden of the energy system.
The characterized results of the four modified scenarios are shown and compared in Table F.18. As seen
in the Table, the incineration scenario yields the highest savings in 12 out of 16 assessed categories. The
CC saving is 164% higher in the incineration scenario compared to Recoma. The Fiskeby and Hündgen
scenarios present the highest savings of FE, LU, and WU. Recoma yields the highest environmental
saving of RD. Figure 8.6 illustrates the differences in CC between the energy mixes for the four scenarios.
The most substantial change is in the incineration scenario. For the other scenarios, recycling will
encompass a saving to the environment concerning CC, in different degrees, depending on the modelled
energy system.
Figure 8.6: Illustration of the CC impact of the four scenarios modelled with the different energy
mixes. Market stands for the Ecoinvent market process, used in the model in the LCIA. Cons.LCA is
the future predictions given by Muñoz & Weidema (2021). Current sys. stands for the current energy
system in 2021, as obtained from IEA (n.d.)
If CC was the only valid impact category, the figure illustrates clearly that it is the incineration scenario
that yields the highest environmental saving. Based on the results presented above, incineration presents
savings in other impact categories as well. However, it will be wrong to make decisions only based on
the past, depicted by the current energy system, and not consider future consequences, depicted in
future scenarios.
==PAGE:93==
CHAPTER 9
Discussion
As seen in this study, the subject of UBC waste management is broad and encompasses many different
aspects. This section outlines and discusses some of the major issues in the study.
The use of BC is expected to increase, especially in food cartons replacing metal cans. However, UBC
is a complex fraction. It is a low-value product, and the requirement for a short treatment time due to
the residues in the waste makes treating it even more complicated. There are still some problems in the
development phase, such as insects in the waste and the deterioration of the material in the summer
heat if it is not sorted and recycled quickly enough.
9.1
Materials and residues
The model’s input for the amount of each material used in BC is based on the literature (Stramarkou
et al., 2021; Pasqualino et al., 2011; Miljøstyrelsen, 2018), but the actual composition is slightly more
varied. For example, Banar & Cokaygil (2008); Schlecht & Wellenreuther (2020), and ACE (2021a)
use somewhat different compositions for the aseptic cartons. Based on the data from Markwardt et al.
(2017) and Meyhoff Fry et al. (2010), the plastic content for the various refrigerated cartons can be
calculated to be 21–36%, which is likely to affect the environmental impact of the UBCs, especially
with the current system and the assumptions that all plastics and aluminum are incinerated, and only
cardboard fibers are recovered.
The different products’ clean weights (baseline) measurements were performed on 70 UBCs. The limited
number of samples may be criticized as insuﬀicient or not covering the whole variety of products on the
Danish market. Although it could have been conducted with more samples, the scope of 35 ECTS for
this study should be considered. Moreover, very accurate weights and distribution of BC types are not
necessarily the better option. Since BC is an evolving market, and product consumption is flexible, the
model should not be too rigid if the goal is to investigate the national waste treatment of BC. There
are also seasonal differences in the types of used cartons. For example, while milk cartons are used
throughout the year, samples can be expected to show a higher incidence of juice, Koldskål (a Danish
dairy product), wine, and popsicles (Sun-lolly and similar products) in the summer, while more cream,
Gløgg wine, and similar products are used in the winter.
Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017) conducted a national study for the Netherlands in which they estimated
national consumption and collection, sorting, and recycling rates. However, their study was much larger
and used more samples and resources to obtain a representative national consumption. Because one
person performed the present study, measuring so many samples from so many municipalities was
impossible. Sampling in this study was done for SS waste from the Copenhagen municipality and RHW
from two municipalities in Zealand, totaling 176 kg of UBC waste. For comparison, in the Dutch study,
33 samples were taken from the different collection schemes in at least 30 Dutch municipalities. Each
sample consisted of 1–2 m3 of waste, with an average weight of 71.6 kg per sample. In total, 2.4 tons
of waste were investigated byThoden van Velzen et al. (2017).
This study investigated and compared the levels of residues in RHW and SS waste streams. It was
found that the batch of UBC collected from RHW (i.e., not source-segregated) contained significantly
more impurities or food waste. The increased residue content may be due to several reasons. First, the
==PAGE:94==
9.1 Materials and residues
74
RHW batch was transported in a truck without a compactor, making the manual sorting process of
non-compacted waste more manageable. The second reason is connected to human behavior. According
to Andreas Kortegaard, the sorting manager at Econet, this could be because much of the food waste
or expired food typically ends up in RHW rather than biowaste. He contended that people do not want
to handle expired food and do not separate it from its packaging, instead throwing it completely into
the RHW. Mogens Thude of AFLD supported this claim but also remarked that food waste is often
disposed of in its packaging. Some throw it into biowaste with the packaging, while most put it in the
RHW.
Figure 9.1: Yogurt cartons found in the SS waste
stream. Some UBCs are cut open and flattened to
remove as much content/residues as possible. This
may indicate engagement, but it could also be an
attempt to reduce food waste.
The scenario analysis has shown that more
residues in the material input make waste treat-
ment less eﬀicient and sustainable.
Thus, con-
sideration should be given to reducing the levels
of residues in cartons. One option is to ask con-
sumers to rinse BCs with water, as proposed by
Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017) and applied by
several Danish municipalities. However, the in-
creased water consumption will affect the LCA
and is very diﬀicult to estimate. Moreover, this
may discourage more residents from sorting their
UBC for recycling since this involves an even big-
ger hassle.
Waste sampling indicates how much residue re-
mains in the BC. However, most studies do not
consider the emptiability of the assessed prod-
uct or the actual level of residues, which can
substantially affect the results, as also shown
in this study.
Accordingly, the environmental
impact of UBC, including residues, can only be
compared to other materials if they also con-
tain residues.
Otherwise, it would be mislead-
ing. Wohner et al. (2019) estimated that residues
were present to varying degrees in every type
of packaging. Therefore, residues should be in-
cluded in the food packaging assessment as they can significantly affect the environmental impact of
waste management. Investigating food loss in packaging may be worthwhile, as its environmental im-
pact is only half the story. Beverage cartons generally have poorer emptiability than other packaging
materials, such as glass and some plastic packaging (Wohner et al., 2019).
The contribution of food packaging on the environment is debated in the literature. According to Simon
et al. (2016), the contribution of packaging is calculated to be up to 45% of the total food value chain.
However, other studies have estimated the contribution of packaging to be considerably lower than
that of food production (Wohner et al., 2019) and that food loss has a more significant impact than
packaging (Silvenius et al., 2011). In their study, Boesen et al. (2019) distinguished between food and
soft drinks, claiming that soft drink packaging accounted for more than half of the carbon footprint,
compared to 10% for food.
Nevertheless, the waste sampling has shown that there is a difference between the technical emptiability
of BCs and human behavior or actual efforts to empty UBCs. As shown in 9.1, some cartons were cut
open and emptied, while others were disposed of with significant residues. Thus, even though technical
emptiability is a crucial factor, human behavior, and resident engagement are just as important, if not
more so.
Changes in consumer behavior require years of campaigning. A Ph.D. dissertation conducted in Den-
mark investigated sorting patterns in Denmark (Katan, 2022). ). It concluded that inconvenience is a
==PAGE:95==
9.2 Collection and sorting
75
major barrier to waste sorting but also that sorting should be normalized and not just be convenient if
aiming to improve sorting. if sorting is to be improved. The example given is glass and paper sorting,
considered “normal” or taken for granted at the household level (Katan, 2022). According to Chen et al.
(2022), ecological cognition, defined as consumers’ basic understanding of the ecological environment
and ecosystem services, influences the willingness to engage in beverage packaging recycling (Chen
et al., 2022). Chen et al. (2022) also emphasized media and publicity’s importance in raising ecological
awareness.
The distribution of BC types used in this study does not necessarily reflect actual consumption but
only what Copenhagen residents choose to sort for recycling. These choices are based on engagement,
environmental awareness, campaigns, and word choice. According to Malene Møhl of the Copenhagen
municipality, awareness campaigns for the new waste fraction initially emphasized milk cartons, which
resulted in milk cartons entering the SS waste stream. A campaign on food cartons resulted in many
more pizza boxes ending up in the waste. Therefore, in December 2022, they launched a campaign
showing typical food cartons, such as soup, sauce, or tomatoes, to increase the source segregation of these
types of UBCs (Møhl, 2022). The example of Copenhagen municipality emphasizes the importance of
communication to raise awareness (Chen et al., 2022). The need for campaigns is also seen at Nordværk,
which has published several videos on social media about the right way to sort these waste fractions
(Nordværk, 2023).
9.2
Collection and sorting
The collection of UBC is still in its infancy in Denmark, which may be one of the reasons for the low
collection rate in 2022. According to Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017), collection matures over time. In
the Netherlands, the collection rate of UBC as a separate collection increased from 16% to 24% within
three years.
However, this may also be due to system expansion rather than resident engagement
(Thoden van Velzen et al., 2017). According to ACE, countries where UBC collection targets have been
in place for some time, such as Belgium and Germany, have higher collection and recycling rates (ACE,
2022b). Thus, it may be a question of the maturity of the collection system as well as quantitative
targets.
According to Miljøstyrelsen (2020b), the collection of UBC is one of the most expensive initiatives to
increase household waste recycling. They estimated the costs of UBC collection to be about DKK
3,600–6,300 per ton of recycled waste, depending on the calculation method1.
One of the goals of the Danish government’s plan for waste management and the circular economy was
to streamline household waste collection throughout the country. This was done to facilitate further
sorting and treatment of waste (Bukholt, 2022) and allow people to sort their waste in exactly the same
way regardless of where they are in Denmark, whether at home or with friends, or at their summer
house. However, this is not the case for UBC or plastic, metal, or glass (Bukholt, 2023). Figure 9.2
shows that the concept of streamlining has not been implemented in the Danish market. The map only
shows the collection schemes for UBC in the different municipalities.
1The costs of UBC collection are estimated at 3,592 DKK of budget per ton of recycled waste. When including societal
costs, the cost is estimated at 6,303 DKK per ton of recycled waste (Miljøstyrelsen, 2020b)
==PAGE:96==
9.3 Recycling
76
Figure 9.2: A map of the 98 Danish municipalities and their UBC collection scheme. The municipal-
ities in the area around Copenhagen and Bornholm are inserted in the map and are not geographically
placed. The data used for this figure can be found in Appendix H.
The map shows 13 Danish municipalities that collect UBC waste in waste stations. However, UBC
waste is not the ideal waste fraction for a waste station because waste collected in waste stations is
generally more inert and suitable for long-term storage at consumers’ homes and in waste stations.
The same idea of streamlining waste collection schemes was proposed by the European Commission
(2022a) for the EU. A map of Europe showing the different collection schemes used in each country for
UBC is shown in Appendix G.
9.3
Recycling
In older studies conducted by the Danish EPA, UBC was not considered a separate fraction but part
of a broader fraction called composite materials (Miljøstyrelsen, 2020b, 2019; Miljøministeriet, 2021b).
The composite materials fraction also included chip bags, coffee capsules, and other packages using
composite materials. Other reports suggested a fraction of contaminated paper and cardboard, such as
pizza boxes, food packaging, and paper towels (Miljøstyrelsen, 2016).
==PAGE:97==
9.3 Recycling
77
However, the decision plan only included the collection and recycling of UBC (Danish Government,
2020). The exclusive focus on UBCs could be because the technological solutions are insuﬀicient to
recycle all the composite materials. Regardless, one of the goals of the EPR system for packaging
waste is to incentivize manufacturers to produce more sustainable and recyclable packaging. Pursuing
sustainable and recyclable packaging will likely lead to different types of packaging.
According to
Schmidt et al. (2022), recycling processes cannot keep up with innovations in multilayer packaging
materials. It could be argued that composite materials are more sustainable because fewer materials
are used. However, for packaging to be recyclable, it is better to replace multilayers with mono-material
solutions (Schmidt et al., 2022).
The Danish EPA has set a target for UBC recycling of 14,000 tons annually by 2025 (Miljøstyrelsen,
2020b), and the collection rate is expected to increase by 4,000 tons per year to reach 20,000 tons by 2025
(Miljøstyrelsen, 2022), which is about 74% of the estimated amount of BC placed on the Danish market
in 2021 (Extr:act, 2023). This is a much higher collection rate than in all other reviewed European
countries, as shown in Table 2.1, except for Germany. It should be noted, however, that the target was
set to meet the EU recycling goals, not from experience. In addition, the results from Miljøstyrelsen
(2020b) showed that the incineration of 1 ton of UBC yields a saving to the environment of -339 kg
CO2 eq., which is a much smaller saving than the -949 kg CO2 eq. saving calculated in the scenario
analysis for the current energy system while recycling the same material results in a saving of -680 kg
CO2 eq. The savings from recycling vary among the three assessed scenarios but range from -11 to -356
CO2 eq. However, as mentioned earlier in the study, LCA results cannot be directly compared because
such an assessment has so many variables and assumptions. One of the main assumptions is residues,
which are not modelled in the EPA’s assessment (Miljøstyrelsen, 2020b, p. 133).
This goal is contended by ACE, which, as a BC producer organization, is interested in showing BC’s
sustainability. According to the ACE impact assessment of EU-wide UBC collection, the Danish collec-
tion rate will reach 20% by business as usual, and 39% in case of EU adopts ACE’s proposed target of
a 90% collection rate by 2030 (ACE, 2022b). Hence, it will take a lot to reach the Danish EPA’s target
of 20,000 tons collected annually. Also, the expectation is that 14,000 tons, or 70% of the collected
waste, will be recycled. This is also a high target that will be hard to achieve due to residues in the BC
and varying sorting eﬀiciencies. For comparison, plastic has been collected for recycling for 12 years in
Copenhagen, and the collection for recycling encompasses approximately 30% of the generated plastic
waste (Boesen et al., 2019).
One way to achieve the collection- and recycling goals set by the Danish EPA is the proposed central
sorting plants of RHW (Findsen, 2020). Rambøll evaluated the matter for the municipalities in Fyn and
concluded that central sorting is more sustainable than source segregation at the household level in 14
out of 18 assessed impact categories (Rambøll, 2022). However, SS waste streams yield cleaner fractions
which are easier to treat, and higher recycling rates (Amskov, n.d.). The concept of central sorting has
been up to debate in recent months (Arve, 2023), and some use the Netherlands and Norway as positive
examples of this type of sorting (Graversen, 2023; Eriksen, 2023). According to Thoden van Velzen et al.
(2017), who investigated UBC recycling in the Netherlands, the overall recycling rate of UBC from RHW
is higher than for SS waste streams; see Table 9.1 for comparison. However, the level of residues is
also higher in UBC recovered from RHW (similar to the results obtained in the waste sampling in this
study), and the organic materials give the recovered fibers a strong odor, considered a quality-limiting
factor for recycling (Thoden van Velzen et al., 2017). Moreover, not all recycling facilities can treat
relatively highly contaminated UBC waste (Thoden van Velzen et al., 2017). Experts in the Danish
EPA project regarding the quality of plastic and UBC disagree with the statement and estimate that
the quality of waste originating in RHW is not reduced compared to SS waste (Miljøstyrelsen, 2021).

Table 9.1 shows that the estimates of sorting and recycling rates by Rambøll (2022) are consistently
higher than the values obtained by Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017). It could be due to many reasons,
system boundaries, assumptions, temporal variation, and improved technology.
Figure 9.3: UBCs from Norway’s central sorting
plant as observed in Recoma’s recycling plant in Swe-
den. From observations, the UBCs do not seem dif-
ferent from UBC from other sorting plants, nor do
they contain visibly more residues
ARI’s observations from the Norwegian central
sorting plant for recyclable waste concluded that
the yield from central sorting is not financially
equivalent to the needed investment since much
of the sorted plastic cannot be recycled (Bentsen,
2022). The article does not conclude specifically
regarding UBC, yet according to Recoma, the
Norwegian UBC recovered from RHW can be
used in their production, see Figure 9.3.
Cur-
rently, the Danish EPA has approved the idea for
a central sorting plant for plastic but withholds
the approval regarding UBC (Bentsen, 2023).
This could be because the presence of UBC short-
ens the lifetime of the combined fraction of plas-
tic and UBC before it is necessary to recycle it.
Several experts agree that the source segregation
of household waste into 10 fractions is too de-
manding of the citizens and that Denmark will
not achieve its recycling goals in this way alone
(Extr:act, 2023; Eriksen, 2023). Others believe
that it will be pedagogically wrong not to require
citizens to sort at home since it improves the en-
vironmental awareness for consumption and re-
source use, especially if one of the overall policy
goals is to reduce waste generation (Danish Gov-
ernment, 2020; European Commission, 2022a).
It is also worth considering that all Danish mu-
nicipalities have invested resources in implement-
ing the 10 waste fractions plan and that central
sorting plants are yet to be built in Denmark.
Therefore, working towards an improved collec-
tion rate based on household source segregation
==PAGE:99==
9.4 LCA limitations
79
is still necessary. Other experts in the field ac-
cept the idea of central sorting of RHW but propose that it will not replace household source segregation
(Bukholt, 2023).
Data about the recycling processes took much work to come by. It may be due to competition, as is
the case in Germany. It could also be for other reasons. Reading through the IFEU reports revealed
that the lack of specific data regarding the recycling processes of UBC is a more common problem
than anticipated. IFEU used data from Fiskeby board in their Nordic report (Markwardt et al., 2017).
For the European LCA, data about recycling processes was obtained from various European recycling
plants in 2004 (Schlecht & Wellenreuther, 2020), which by now is outdated. More transparency should
be encouraged in order to make representative studies.
The current recycling schemes for UBC, whether fiber recovery or conversion to construction boards,
are considered downcycling. The fibers from the UBC are supposed to be of high quality (Agamuthu
& Visvanathan, 2014), but this type of fibers can only be recycled a few times, 3.5 times, according to
Pivnenko et al. (2016), since the quality declines rapidly with every new loop. The PE-Al fraction is
also downcycled since it is usually not separated but either landfilled, incinerated for energy recovery,
or shredded and used for construction. Even in Palurec, the new German recycling plant for the PE-Al
fraction, the separation is incomplete, and some aluminum fractions remain in the recovered plastic.
This type of recycling lies lower in the waste hierarchy than other types of recycling since BC will never
be circular, nor could be recycled for food packaging materials.
Recycling should not be seen as the golden solution since even a 100% recycling rate may not result
in emission reduction because of the environmental burden of the production of secondary materials
(Simon et al., 2016). Supporting this, the LCIA in Section 7.2 shows that recycling does not perform
better than incineration in all impact categories.
Lastly, there is a question of recycling capacity. The current sorting and recycling plants for paper
and UBC cannot accommodate a 100% collection rate. According to Zero Waste Europe (2020), the
recycling capacity in the UK is 25,000 tons, while 60,000 tons of material are being placed on the market.
The German recycling plants also have a limited capacity, and it should be investigated whether there is
suﬀicient capacity to accommodate the Danish goals. However, Fiskeby boards are planning to expand
their production. A new recycling plant dedicated to UBC is being planned in Sweden to be used
as a Nordic hub, so capacity should be a manageable factor for the collection and sorting of UBC in
Denmark.
9.4
LCA limitations
The final element of the LCA is the conclusions, limitations, and recommendations. Since this study
encompasses more than just an LCA, the conclusion will be made for the whole study rather than just
the LCA.
There are some limitations to the assessment made in this study.
This study treated food cartons in bulk due to the significant volume, size, and product variation.
Seeing as the use of BC for food is expected to increase, it would have been more accurate to analyze
them separately and not in bulk. For example, tomato sauce could have been separated since it was
one of the food cartons with the highest occurrence.
It would make more sense to obtain data regarding impurities and clean weight from the same mea-
surements and using the same precision scale. For the measurements conducted in ARC and Econet’s
sorting hall, bulkier scales were used, which made for less precise estimates. However, the resources
including access to equipment were limited due to the scale of the study.
Unlike the study made by Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017), the collection rates were not calculated sep-
==PAGE:100==
9.4 LCA limitations
80
arately for the different collection schemes, PD, PMD, and separate collection2. It would be interesting
to evaluate the effectiveness of each collection scheme, especially in light of the government’s idea of
making the collection streamlined across Denmark.
Figure 9.4:
Impurities found in a sample of SS
waste stream sorted by the NIR scanner at the test
sorting plant at ARC, Copenhagen
In this study, the only impurities considered were
the organic ones left in the cartons. Other impu-
rities were not considered, nor was it considered
that the UBCs, in most cases, are not separately
collected but with plastic and sometimes metal.
The use of the NIR scanner minimizes the level of
impurities in the sorted waste, but the five sam-
ples from SS waste streams, measured both for
quantity and weight, showed an average rate of
4% other impurities per sample containing other
types of plastic, cardboard, and non-recyclable
waste; see Figure 9.4 for an example. The level
of impurities may be reduced in a commercial
sorting plant, but impurities exist in all plants
and must be dealt with appropriately.
The energy use in the Fiskeby board plant in Swe-
den is primarily based on solid fuels and innova-
tive biofuel. However, the modelling is limited
to Ecoinvent and Easetech processes. Therefore,
the new fuels used by Fiskeby could not be ap-
propriately modeled, which put the Fiskeby scenario at a disadvantage, as it is probably more envi-
ronmentally beneficial than modelled. Nevertheless, it is assumed that the combustion of the fuel is
modelled and accounted for in the emissions from the plant. However, the production of fuel could
make a difference.
Additionally, plastic and aluminum were not recycled in the assessed scenarios, except for Recoma,
where UBCs were shredded completely. The assumption that the PE-Al fraction is incinerated in the
fibers recovery plants can be an additional disadvantage for these scenarios since several studies show
that the recycling of all the materials is better than only recovering the fibers (Sund, 2020; Xie et al.,
2013; Varžinskas et al., 2012).
Lastly, the substitution of materials in the Recoma scenario is adjusted to fit the system boundaries,
which is the substitution of primary material and not finished products.
Therefore, the UBCs are
assumed to substitute virgin pulpwood and plastic as filling for the construction boards. The use of
recycled UBC, a low-value material with a short lifetime but with potential, and pulping reject3 is
one of the reasons behind the production.
However, this substitution is artificial since the boards
would probably not have been produced if they had been made of virgin materials. A more realistic
substitution would have been for equivalent construction boards made of virgin materials, which would
have yielded a higher environmental saving.
2PD is the Dutch equivalent of plastic and UBC. PMD is the Dutch equivalent of metal, plastic, and UBC.
3The PE-Al fractions of the UBC waste, as well as cardboard fibers that were not recovered (Khan et al., 2021)
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 10
Conclusions
This study aimed to investigate the waste management of UBC in Denmark and determine which waste
treatment method is the most environmentally sustainable. This was done by answering the following
questions.
What is the current state of UBC waste treatment in Denmark since implementing the
new waste strategy?
As of March 2023, 69 out of 98 Danish municipalities have implemented household collection of UBC,
using four different collection schemes, where the most common is plastic and UBC waste collected
together. 13 additional municipalities have begun the collection of UBC, but in waste stations, which
is not ideal for this type of waste. The remaining 16 have yet to begin the UBC collection. The Danish
UBC waste is sent to 8 different sorting plants in Denmark or Germany. The recycling processes take
place in Germany or Sweden.
What were the material flows of UBCs in Denmark in 2022?
After implementing the new management plan, the MFA was used to determine the material flows of
UBC in Denmark. A data analysis conducted by the author prior to this study estimated that the
generated UBC waste in Denmark is at 32,393 tons and contains approximately 20% residues. The
main part of UBC was still incinerated in 2022. Based on the data from stakeholders, 1648 tons of
UBC waste were collected in 2022, yielding a national collection rate of 5% of the generated UBC waste.
Based on the average municipal collection rate in 2022, the potential national UBC collection is 2,615
tons annually, but it could be between 371 and 17,018 tons. An overall recycling rate was estimated to
be 2.5% of the generated waste, but the recycling rate is highly uncertain.
Which cartons do Danish consumers use, and which materials do they contain?
Waste sampling was used to determine the distribution of BC types in the waste and to estimate the level
of residues. 5853 UBCs from RHW and SS waste streams were used to determine the distribution of
BC types in the different waste streams and the corresponding level of residues in each type. Sampling
showed a substantial difference between weight and quantity concerning the occurrence of milk and
yogurt cartons. Milk cartons showed the highest occurrence among UBC regarding weight and quantity.
Food cartons were more common in RHW than SS waste but were not analyzed further due to the
considerable variation in this category.
The levels of residues in the UBCs were compared for the two waste streams. The results showed that
UBCs from SS waste generally weigh less than UBC from RHW. 35 BC types were compared for average
weight. Eight UBC types from SS waste showed an average weight lower by more than 50% compared
to the equivalent type from RHW.
70 additional UBCs were used to establish a baseline for the BC type with the highest occurrence in
the waste sampling. Weighted averages were used to estimate a level of residues for each BC category
and an overall level of residues of 16-21%.
The MFA and waste sampling results were used to model the LCA.
What is the environmental impact of different UBC waste treatment methods?
The LCA assessed four scenarios, three for recycling and one for incineration, which is considered BAU
in this study. The results of the LCIA show that each scenario yields the best results in several impact
==PAGE:102==
10 Conclusions
82
categories. The Recoma scenario yields the largest environmental savings concerning CC, IR, and RDfos.
Concerning CC, the Recoma scenario yields an environmental savings of -280 kg CO2 eq. per ton UBC
waste, including residues. The Fiskeby and Hündgen scenarios also show a saving to the environment,
though to a lesser extent than Recoma, of -134 and -103 kg CO2 eq., respectively.
In contrast, the incineration scenario shows a burden to the environment concerning CC. Nevertheless,
the incineration scenario yields the best results in 8 out of 16 impact categories. Generally, the different
scenarios yield savings to the environments in the different impact categories. The Fiskeby and Hündgen
scenarios are responsible for most of the burdens from the waste treatment of UBC but also for significant
savings in FE. Moreover, except for the FE and ET, most of the normalized results of the scenarios are
on a scale of -0.25 to 0.05 PE.
The contribution analysis of the four scenarios showed that the background processes, substituting
materials and energy, substantially affect the results. The scenario analysis showed that the modelled
energy system greatly impacted the results.
Out of the reported LCIA results and within the uncertainties presented, The Recoma scenario i.e.,
recycling UBC waste into construction boards, seems to be the most environmentally sustainable option
of the assessed methods. It shows environmental savings in 15 out of 16 assessed impact categories.
The environmental burden of the scenario is for ozone depletion and is estimated to be 1.1 · 10−4 PE.
What are the data gaps for the current systems, and what is needed for a more compre-
hensive understanding of the waste management of UBCs in Denmark?
The collection and recycling of UBC in Denmark are still relatively new and the literature is mainly based
on literature review and experiences from other European countries. Data gaps should be identified to
further investigate the waste management of UBC.
This study uses a novelty approach in the Danish literature by analysing residues in the beverage
packaging material and assessing the entire environmental impact of UBC waste. Nevertheless, for
some of the processes, data gaps were identified.
The LCI inventory used for the assessment was
obtained primarily from involved stakeholders. The data is therefore not verified and, in most cases,
limited to one sorting or recycling plant, limiting the representativeness of the assessment. Especially
the recycling scenarios could have been modelled more accurately. While detailed data were provided
by Fiskeby board and from ARC, data from Germany was limited to the sorting processes. Recoma
also provided a limited dataset, but that could also be because of the short operation time.
The lack of data regarding the recycling processes in Germany resulted in modelling the recycling on
data from Fiskeby board. This was not ideal since the technologies are assumed to be different.
The concept of converting UBC waste to construction boards, as depicted by Recoma sounds promising.
This method of recycling can use the UBC waste in its entirety, as well as reject from other recycling
plants. Nevertheless, more accurate measurements of emissions are needed in order to have a proper
comparison of the different recycling scenarios.
Further development and research of the waste management of UBC can lead in many different direc-
tions. Further analysis of the residues in the UBCs could contribute to a more accurate evaluation of
the environmental impact of the different scenarios. Analysis of the level of residues as a function of
the BC’s volume and the concept of emptiability can contribute to better design and, hopefully, less
food waste. Wider evaluation of recycling methods and the assessment of other available sorting plants
can make the assessment more representative of Denmark. Lastly, it would be interesting to see what
will happen to the BCs and to waste management of the fraction once EPR is implemented in 2025.

APPENDIX A
System boundaries
Figure A.1 illustrates the whole life cycle of the beverage cartons.
Figure A.1: The full life cycle for beverage cartons, including excluded life cycle stages. The excluded
life cycle stages are illustrated based on Schlecht & Wellenreuther (2020). The figure shows an overview
of the three possible EOL scenarios assessed in this study
As shown in Figure A.1, the full life cycle of BC contains the material productions of the different
elements in the BC, the packaging (the actual beverage cartons), production and filling, distribution,
and product use. It does not illustrate the production of the packaging of BC (secondary packaging)
nor the production of the filling. The excluded life cycle stages are marked grey to make it clear.
==PAGE:113==
APPENDIX B
LCI inventory
The following chapter will document the modelling of the different LCA scenarios. It includes specific
amounts, when possible, and processes in EASETECH or Ecoinvent used.
B.1
Material composition
The materials used as input to the system or the reference flow are modelled on the waste sampling of
SS waste from Copenhagen municipality. In the waste sampling, it was estimated that 72% of the UBCs
are refrigerated and 28% are aseptic. The level of residues varies among the different carton types, but
a weighted average resulted in 15.7% residues. The numbers were then normalized using Microsoft excel
to reach 100%. The model is made for 1 ton of UBC, including residues, and the material composition
is shown in Table B.1
Table B.1: The materials used in the reference flow based on the waste sampling and the references
used from EASETECH to model each of the materials

B.2
Collection and transport
The collection process and distance for all four scenarios are modelled identically, with the same distance
for each, to the incineration plant, transfer station, and sorting plant.
The waste from the transfer stations and the Danish sorting plants is packed and baled before the
transport, and a different type of truck is used for longer distances.
The starting point for shipping abroad is Odense C, and the distances are calculated for the selected
plants using google maps. The transportation distances and the Ecoinvent processes used in the models
are shown in Table B.2.

B.3
Transfer stations
The transfer stations are only used for baling and packing the waste that is sorted in Germany, to
make transport more eﬀicient. It is assumed that electricity is used for the baling machine and that
the bales are packed with steel wire and packaging film made of plastic. The electricity process used
is the market process from Ecoinvent for Denmark. Other energy mixes were assessed in the scenario
analysis. The operation managers in AFLD and ARC provided data regarding the packing and baling
processes. The electricity use was given by ARC (2022) as an interval, converted to 5.2-6.5 kWh. The
higher use was chosen to be modelled. The materials used and the respective Ecoinvent processes are
shown in Table B.3

B.4
Loss of residues
It was assumed that some of the residues in the UBCs were extracted from the cartons during the
collection due to the compactor in the collection truck. Some of the residues were also lost during the
baling process. These assumptions were confirmed by the operation managers in AFLD and ARC and
were quantified by Thoden van Velzen et al. (2017) in a study in the Netherlands. The conservative
estimation of 8% loss during transportation and baling were used in this study.
It was assumed that 3% of the loss occurred in the collection process and 5% in the baling process due
to the increased pressure.
It is assumed that the residues left in the collection trucks and the transfer station for the baling process
are collected and sent to incineration. The loss of 3% occurred in all the scenarios but does not appear
in the incineration model since both streams are eventually incinerated.
The loss of 5% occurred in all the recycling scenarios and is assumed to be incinerated in Denmark
since the collection and baling occur in Denmark for all scenarios.
B.5
Incineration
Incineration of the UBC is the baseline of this study and the traditional way to dispose of UBCs in
Denmark. Data regarding energy recovery, emission, and residues were obtained from Bisinella et al.
(2021). The baseline scenario for incineration in the article is used in this study. Since the article is
made to model municipal solid waste, it was slightly modified, and the metal emissions were omitted
from the process-specific emission.
The energy recovery eﬀiciency is modelled as 25.4% for electricity and 72% for heat; the remainder is
assumed to be degraded (Bisinella et al., 2021). The input to the incineration plant is modelled using
the processes and quantities shown in Table B.4. The electricity used is the marginal energy, and the
heat is modelled after the example in the supplementary material (Bisinella et al., 2021).

B.6 Sorting of UBC

B.6
Sorting of UBC
The sorting occurs in Denmark and Germany, where the UBCs are mechanically separated from the
remainder of the waste from that collection, plastic and metal. Based on the data provided by Nordværk
and Combineering for Hündgen Entsorgungs, the sorting processes are modelled for energy use and
baling and packing materials.
Table B.8 shows the Ecoinvent processes used to model the sorting
processes in Denmark and in Germany. Manual work is not modelled in this study. The LDPE plastic
wrap is only modelled for sorting in Denmark since in Hündgen Entsorgungs only uses steel wire. Steel
wire is assumed to be the best Ecoinvent process fit to the wire used in Nordværk.

B.7
Recycling
There are two types of secondary recycling modelled in this study: recovery of cardboard fibers and
conversion of UBCs into construction boards.
The data regarding the recycling processes in Fiskeby board are propriety material and will not be shown
here but only in the confidential appendix. Similarly, the calculations for the construction boards and
how much of each material can be substituted will be presented there. The substitution factor from
UBC to secondary material is given as 0.9 (Schlecht & Wellenreuther, 2020) and modelled as such. It
is assumed that the reject from the recycling processes is incinerated in Sweden with energy recovery.
The data regarding the use of UBC in construction board production are obtained from Recoma (2022).
The residues of the production are re-fed into the system to be used in new boards, and therefore
further management of residues is not modelled.
The substitution of the materials is based on an
estimate regarding the fraction of plastic and cardboard in the boards. It is assumed that pulpwood
has the same density as the UBC mass filling.
The data regarding energy use, the substitution of
materials from both types of recycling, as well as management of residues can be seen in Table B.9.

F.4.2
Comparison to the current energy system
Table F.16 shows the characterized results for the Incineration and Recoma scenarios, both in the
original model and using the current energy system, as obtained from IEA (n.d.) and with a modified
heat system based on Bisinella et al. (2021). The results are shown for each model, and the relative
change is shown using colors to indicate whether the change contributes to savings or to the burdens
to the environment. Table F.17 shows the results in an identical way to the Fiskeby and Hündgen
scenarios.

APPENDIX G
UBC recycling in Europe
Figure G.1 illustrates the different collection schemes used in the European countries for the collection
of UBC. The map is from 2021, and Denmark is registered as a country without UBC collection. The
variation in collection schemes is one of the reasons for the proposal of streamlining the collection
schemes across the EU (European Commission, 2022a)
Figure G.1: The map gives an overview of the European countries and the different collection schemes
used for UBC in each country. The figure is taken from ACE (2021a).

==PAGE:153==
APPENDIX I
Excel files
Clean weight measurements can be seen in the file ”Clean weight measurements”. The samples were
weighed before and after the cleaning process. The relative change was found and used as the level of
residues in each BC type.
Comparison tables can be seen in the file ”Comparison tables”. The data in the file is used to make
Table 5.7 and Table 5.8
Data and results from waste sampling can be seen in the files ”SS waste sampling” and ”RHW waste
sampling”. The data in these files are used in Section 5.2.
The perturbation analysis and data quality assessment can be seen in the file ”Perturbation analysis”.
The data in these files are used in Section 8.4.
==PAGE:154==
APPENDIX J
Data analysis of UBC in
household waste
Before the thesis writing period, between August- September 2022, a special course of 5 ECTS was held
to perform data analysis on Econet’s waste analyses regarding food and beverage cartons in Danish
household waste. The report is attached as a separate file named ”Data analysis of Food and Beverage
cartons in Danish households waste”. The report includes two excel appendices which are also included
in the external appendices folder.
